"Regarding the treatment of plagues."
"I have already said in the division on the treatment of plague what was necessary for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with more severity and medicines according to their degree."
"Therefore, I say that plague is differentiated according to what part of the body the plague affects and according to the location on which the plague appears."
"And the causes for which plague often occurs are such as injury by stone, or incision by sword or knife, or piercing with lance, club, arrow, or other similar causes."
"But plague that occurs in the different parts of the body are such as plague that affects the head, neck, chest, belly, thigh, and similar to those of the limbs."
"And I am recounting the treatment of some plague because this reasoning and rule apply to other plague as well."
"And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the memory of those that are compound will come at the beginning of the third chapter."
"Therefore, I say that when a simple head wound occurs and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from an injury by stone or similar, and the chain has broken only on one side and it is large and you suspect that there may be an abscess forming later on that patient, then immediately perform trepanation of the cephalic vein and flebotomy; do not delay this. And extract from that blood according to the quantity of its virtue if no other cause prohibits flebotomy, especially if little blood comes out of the wound."
"And the flebotomy should be on the opposite side from where the wound is located; and keep the patient from being hit, drinking wine or strong drinks, and eating heavy foods."
"But if due to minuteness you prohibit any cause, then reduce the food and do not approach it with meat or wine."
"And pause over the wound if an abscess forms later on; cut off the hair around it and apply hot oil or wine in which has been steeped some styptic substance."
"But if you are sure of an abscess forming later on, then pause over the wound while it is still fresh with its blood and before the air has changed that powder which I will recommend to you appropriately."
"And if the air has already changed it, then pause over the abscess until it is healed."
"But if the wound is large or it is from an incision by sword or similar and the edges are not adapted for pus, then adapt those edges with sutures according to what I will recommend regarding the suture of wounds."
"But if the wound is already exposed to bone and membrane, and there is only a small suspension of it, then incise that and appropriately treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh appropriately in place of chain; and command the patient to be fed on foods that are coarse: heads of cabbage and similar ones until it is composed."
"And if you find putrefaction in the chain and it does not adhere to the bone, then incise all that is rotting and throw that out, and appropriately treat it."
"But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur and it is not severed by medicines, then inquire about the artery; and if you find that it has not been incised, then incise that with a spatula or ligate it. And if necessity provokes that what we have said does not conform, then cauterize until the blood is severed."
"And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is light. Therefore, you powder those with this powder, for it solidifies that before the air changes it."
"And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of sesame one part, dragon's blood three parts, and un-extinguished incense five parts; grind it all together and sift it, and with it fill the wound, and constrain it well until it adheres to it, and coagulate it with the blood."
"And appropriately constrain that from above with powders and leave it; do not remove it while it is like this until it becomes an abscess because the medicine does not eradicate it before it is healed."
"And sometimes you can use just cautery when you do not have incense and dragon's blood available."
"During great plagues, perform this operation: place and tighten them over those areas; these recent plague sores have not been altered by the air."
"And if the air has altered them, especially in summer time, it is not intended to use this powder. Instead, treat those with the remedy we mentioned earlier: apply a poultice made from honey and flour mixed with water or apply an ointment made from these ingredients. Also treat them with another cure until they are healed."
"And if there happens to be a small bone fracture during the plague, then do this: extract it using gestures."
"And you should say that there is no cure for skull fractures in this case."
"Regarding plague of the neck:"
"But if the plague happens in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between that and simple head plague."
"But if the nerves or arteries of the neck are cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done except to avoid treatment with anything that causes vehement constriction, such as zinc and vitriol. These things resemble them in color and can cause paralysis." "And do not apply any treatment for plague caused by heat, because the substance of nerves is hot and their continuation is with the brain, which is the source of all members."
"And if it is treated with light medicines, such as washing with sweet water, sometimes mixed with rose or non-rose oil, and turpentine when washed with sweet water, and other stones ground in this way, and moistened."
"But if the plague is large, then stitches should be applied, or the lips of it closed, and afterwards treat it with powders until it is healed."
"But if there is depth in the plague or it has happened at a low point that has become corrupted, then perforate it where it is most pressed."
"But if an artery has been cut in the plague and there is bleeding, then separate or dry it out, ligate it, or cauterize it if necessary to provoke it."
"But if the vertebrae of the neck have been injured or all, and if the veins of the neck are saved, then sew up the lips of the wound over the spine of the neck and do not touch that; afterwards equalize it and reduce it to its natural figure, and then strengthen it with decent strapping, leaving it for a few days; afterwards treat it with things that have constriction and excitation until it is healed."
"But if the plague has already reached the bone and caused damage, then percuss it, and if there are fragments of bone in it, remove all that you can; and what cannot be removed, leave it until the plague putsrefies, separates, and its inflammation is alleviated."
"And if the plague is fresh, then powder it with that powder which I have recommended to you." "But if there is a fistula in the plague, then treat it with what is remembered above in its chapter."
"If it is a wound from a lance or a knife and the depth of it is not known, then consider whether there is wind coming out of the plague when the patient breathes; if so, know that the plague is mortal."
"And if it is not deep and the plague is fresh with its own blood, then pause in it for the first hour with the powder recommended above, and do not strengthen it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of it and does not return to the heart and aggravate the illness, but rather pause in it with attractive ointment, and cleanse it twice a day."
"And if the ointment is not available to you, then pause in the opening of the plague with cotton moistened so that it may absorb what comes out from the humidities; and make sure that the patient sleeps on the plague so that what is collected in it may flow."
"But if three days or more have passed since the plague and no spasms or palpitations of the heart or constriction of the lung occur in the patient, and all other conditions are good, then know that the plague is saved; and therefore treat it with poultices and the other treatment until it is healed."
"But if its healing is delayed and a long time has passed, then know that there is already a fistula; therefore treat it according to its chapter."
"And if the plague is from an incision made with a razor or a knife and is simple on the surface of the flesh or the back, then treat it with what has been mentioned above about suture, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small."
"But if there has already been damage to the bone and small pieces have formed in it, then turn the plague inside out and try to extract those pieces if they are separated." "But if they are not separated, then leave them until the plague putsrefies, as extraction of them will be alleviated."
"And for other accidental wounds in the other members, their treatment is the same as what has been said above."
"About wounds of the belly."
"The condition that occurs in the belly is either great, or small, or moderate."
"Request for disruption is made difficult by another manner, and so it seems that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not immediately return them to their own place, because they break and swell up so that the introduction of them becomes difficult."
"And therefore the best disruption is middle, because in that one the reduction of the bowel is not difficult, as it is difficult in the two species before mentioned."
"And know that suture of the belly is done in four manners, but two sutures are common that are useful in sutures of the other wounds of the body."
"And the two other proper sutures communicate with others, but however they are more proper in the suture of the belly."
"And I recount to you each one with exposition and declaration."
"Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some cause of the bowel comes out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two manners: either by the smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when the bowel has come swelling up because of the coldness of the air."
"And therefore when this is so, it is necessary that you scarify that until you immerse a sponge or wet cloth in hot water only, or you take it in water with soap, and cypers, and spice, and embrocations with that the swelling may be resolved."

"Regarding the instrument, see its form:"
"The one end of it is cut acute and the other not acute, and the extremity is subtle but not too much so as to be like a spatha, rather let it be blunt somewhat."
"And therefore when the wound is enlarged and the bowel comes out, then its reduction should be according to its natural form, in its proper place if that is possible, because it is better."
"But when the disruption is large and it is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on his back and raise his hips up as high as his head; but if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should raise his head and place him on his side."
"And similarly if the disruption is in one of the two sides of the belly, then you should always have your intention and consideration towards this so that the part where the wound is may be raised up more than the other."
"And thus it is necessary that you administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds."
"And in small wounds, pause what is prepared for you, and let it be that the minister between your hands is between the most skilled ones, who will hold the disruption entirely with his hand and adjust the lips of it, and let him uncover from this to that which secures some cause or other."
"And this is the common way of suturing: one that you take the needle or count of needles according to the quantity of the breadth of the wound; and then leave it at the extremity of the disruption from the ring finger, and thread the needle through once, but do not put a knot on both ends of the cord, with two ends of the ciphac which is left the cord of the parts within, until you have made that penetrate that part; and you already have four orders done."
"And then thread with double thread around the needle, and sometimes two parts together, and then join both lips and the wound by a decent joining; and then leave the thickness of the finger and place another needle in that, and then tie it with thread like the first one with the needle; and do not cease doing this until you have completed all the contraction of the wound."
"And let the needles be medium-sized between thickness and subtlety, for thin needles cut through the flesh too lightly; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the cord, therefore it is necessary that they be medium in thickness and subtlety."
"And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed too much in both ends of the cord, so that the flesh is not cut lightly and the wound closes before its consolidation."
"And do not make stitches too long lest the wound be hindered from healing."
"And also cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient during his sleep; and you will place over the stitches powder spread out from every part, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave those until you know that the wound has already healed."
"And this kind of stitching with needles in whatever way is more suitable for small wounds, because it suffices to use one needle or two or around those."
"But the second stitching is like this: it is that you administer with stitching the four ends, of the cord and both ends of the ciphac, at once, with a needle in which there is thread wrapped around it in subtlety and thickness."
"And then when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, reduce the needle from that same part from which you began, so that the thread draws back from the uppermost part of the wound to the lower part, so that the stitching is like stitching cloth in which the cloths are stretched."
"And place between each stitch the thickness of a little finger."
"And this stitching is known to men."
"And with such stitching I closed a wound that had happened to a man in his belly, which had been caused by a knife, and the disruption of the wound was more than a palm, and it extended from the bowels around two palms of the bowel of the middle place, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after twenty-four hours since the bowel had been forced out by the wound."

"But I will tell two kinds of stitching proper according to the narration of the Word."
"And it is that you begin with stitching the cord, and then enter the needle from the outer part to the inner part."

"Once you have inserted the needle, then insert it again in the same point of the skin from without to within; and let go of the extremity of the sinew that is on this side, and insert the needle into the other extremity of the within part towards without."

"And do this once or twice, and do it once again inside out as you see fit according to that example."
"And the summary of this manner of suture is that you should use suturing with silk thread, so that you pierce the sinew once from the side of the cord's extremity and leave it for another time to complete it."
"But the second species of suture which Doctor Guainerio describes explicitly is that you should pierce it according to semi-circumference, as some physicians do. Thus they join each part to its corresponding part conveniently by nature; and they apply one extremity of it to another extremity and the other extremity of the mirror to another extreme, and this is done according to how you recommend."
"And that you leave the needle in the extremity of the mirror near the part outside, and make it penetrate into the inside of that part, leaving two extremities of the sinew; and then turn the needle around and make it penetrate from outside to inside at both extremities of the sinew."
"And then turn those loops around and make each loop penetrate from inside out into the outside of that part at both extremities of the mirror, except for those on the opposite side."
"And this manner of suture is better than common light suture, and it is the suture which was previously mentioned."

"When rupture occurs in the intestines and is small, it may be possible for some people to recover. For example, I have seen a man who was struck on his right side by an arrow, causing a wound in his stomach that had healed over time but still had a fistula opening. He was able to manage it with medications and diet."
"So I advised him to cease trying to heal the wound further once it was clear that it would not be cured. Instead, he should focus on cleaning and bandaging the area so that it could consolidate."
"And I saw that once the wound had healed over, he suffered no further illness due to the injury in his stomach. He did not complain about any discomfort and maintained good health by eating well, drinking moderately, bathing regularly, and exercising."
"Some experienced practitioners have said that when a small rupture occurs in the intestines, it should be treated in this manner: take figs with large heads and place one on each side of the wound. Then wrap another fig around both sides of the wound so that its mouth is open. When it absorbs onto those sides and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, as it adheres but does not dissolve."
"And then place another fig near the first one, and continue this process with figs until all of the wound is covered according to its size."
"And then reduce and sew up the wound. Leave those attached heads inside the intestine until it has healed, without causing any harm or discomfort."
"And it is possible to suture the intestine with a fine thread made from the gut of an animal, as it is more like a needle. You should take the end of this gut thread and make sure it is well-scraped, then tie one end of it around the end of the intestine with a double knot. Then pass that tied end through a needle and suture the intestine back together at the site of the rupture."
"And this method of suturing with figs is merely a matter of trust and hope."
"But if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then suturing alone will not suffice, as it will only result in a fine scar without healing the wound completely."
"On treating fistulas and abscesses:"
"Know that all plague and boil when it empties and becomes ancient, and is made into an ulcer, and is not consolidated and gives off a bad smell at all times, then in any member it is universally called fistula, which we call azukem."
"According to truth, fistula is a hard, rough, white nodosity that causes no pain and has concavity like the concavity of chicken bone; therefore it is called that swollen tumor, which means chicken bone."
"And in some people it is moist, which gives off a bad smell; but when the moisture is cut out in certain hours, it becomes firm." "Sometimes this moisture is thick, scant, coarse, and subtle."
"I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and the treatment of them with medicines in the division."
"And therefore I will tell you about the treatment of fistula which is done by operation with my hands."

"And therefore those of these fistulas that are according to this manner are difficult to heal and do not receive treatment; occupation with them is hard work and ignorance."

"On the treatment of sinus conditions and the quality of enemas with medicines."
"When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends until it makes collection of pus, and then it bursts and perforates, and the pus comes out from it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the cord that is above it appears thus like a thin cloth, and no corruption has reached behind, and does not impress on bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula."
"But if the space extends within where corruption impresses on some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus."
"And its treatment is to incise that cord entirely as it appears, especially when it is thin and made like cloth, and you ensure that it does not adhere to the place by its corruption."
"But if you hope that the cord will adhere and that corruption has not yet reached behind succession, and there is secretion from the face of the cord, then treat it with an enema."
"And if you consider the sinus and then it is large and the arm that extends has a foul odor, then apply an enema with Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, sour wine and cinnamon powder, mixed equally in one vessel and cooked over the fire until it reaches the consistency of honey."
"And then you take from it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and apply the enema with that to the sinus, and strengthen the opening of it, and leave the medicine in it for the duration of two hours."
"And then remove that with a probe; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the corruption is removed."
"And sometimes an enema is applied with water of ashes when this medicine is not present. And if you take the ash of grapevines, put it on the water, and then boil that and apply the enema with it until you are satisfied that the sinus is cleansed."
"And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the water of ashes, then apply an enema with water and honey mixed together three times, and also apply one with wine and honey mixed, because the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is surplus of astringency and dryness in the wine."
"And when you do this several times and are satisfied that the sinus has been removed, then apply an enema with what causes flesh to grow in it."
"And if you take some diaphoretic unguent and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine, or apply the enema with other medicines and unguents that have the properties that have already been written in the treatise on unguents."
"And if the opening of the sinus is narrow and cannot receive the cannula of the enema, then dilate it gradually with a small iron probe, and pause in it with warm water wrapped in Egyptian unguent or basilicon unguent until it is dilated."
"And similarly, if the opening is wide, then gather its lips with a suture and leave in it the quantity that is between the enema without addition."
"And if the opening of the sinus from which the arm emerges is elevated above the parts above it, then close that in the pus below its place so that the arm may run down easily, because when the arm is compressed by the narrowness of the sinus, flesh can be generated there."
"And if it is not possible to close the sinus in the pus below according to what you wish, then prepare the member with preparation so that the arm may run out of it lightly as it appears to you and that there is no cause of the arm retained in it."
"Remove that which is over the opening of the sinus, which removes it at all hours so that it does not hinder what is adjusted in it regarding the arm." "And so that you may know if what runs out from the sinus of the arm is much or little, whether it is digested or undigested:"

"But if the sinus already has some subtle virus, then do not despair of its adhesion, because its healing is approaching, for there are some people in whom the generation of flesh takes a long time in their wounds, and among those there are also those in whom it happens conversely."
"And if the arm is undigested from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then adjust drying medicines, and if the medicines are humid in their substance and virtue, they become dry like lion unguent when there is an excess of addition of zein."

"Regarding cutting for worms generated under the skin, which is called bovine ailment."

"Regarding cutting for the ailment called nafir."
"This ailment is called 'nafir' in our land and it is a pain that appears in some limbs and also changes from limb to limb."
"And you have already seen it described as I will tell you." "I was called to see a woman who was ill in a certain village. When I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation on the vein of the elbow joint. After about an hour, I saw that inflammation move like a worm crawling towards me, but more slowly than it should be, as if it were alive silver flowing from place to place."
"And then the pain moved to that area and fixed itself in the muscle for about an hour."
"And then it ran through the other arm until it reached the other elbow joint."
"And she told me that she felt it moving throughout her body as I saw it. She was amazed by the way it moved and its speed from limb to limb." "And I had already seen something similar in my patients before that time, although I have not seen many cases where the pain moved from limb to limb." "But I did not see it happen like this in other patients as it happened with this woman."
"But I thought that this was because the woman was elderly, with dry skin and visible veins, which is why we could feel that moving wind."
"It is necessary that it does not appear according to this description in those who are delicate and have moist skin with hidden veins."

"Regarding the extraction of arrows."
"Arrows are diverse according to their species and the parts of the body where they strike. Their diversity is such that some have large points, others small, concave or blunt ones. Some are solid, while others have three edges, tongues, or barbs."
"And the signs of a wound to the brain are when an arrow strikes it and passes through the bone, causing shock, darkness before the eyes, dizziness, redness in the face, inflammation of the tongue, spasms and confusion of the mind, vomiting of bile, difficulty speaking or loss of speech, and there is white fluid resembling dust issuing from the wound, with the surrounding area appearing wet like flesh."
"And therefore if you see these signs, then abstain from treating and extracting the arrow, unless it is superficial."
"The signs of the arrow when it enters the heart are such that it is near the left nipple, and it feels as if something hard has been placed there, not a hollow cause. And strong and sudden movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and flow of black bloody plasma, and that chill in the extremities, sweat, faintness; thus you would know that death descends suddenly without warning."
"And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are: frothy bloody spittle from the mouth, and vessels near the neck become congested, and the color of the invalid is changed, and they expire with a great sigh or gasp for breath."
"And if the arrow pierces the pleura which is in the chest, there will be pain near the little ribs at once, and a great difficulty of breathing with violent pain and sighing, and all the members are paralyzed."
"And if the arrow pierces the stomach, immediately there is a strong flow of undigested food from the mouth."
"And if the arrow pierces the belly and is stuck there, some wound or tear of the flesh or bowel, which has been broken, will occur, and there is little hope for recovery from this, even if the arrow were extracted."
"And if the arrow pierces the bladder, urine flows out immediately, and there follows inflammation of the surrounding parts, and strong pain is felt at once, so that you will know it to be a fatal wound."
"But the other limbs such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, arms, and ribs, spine, collarbone, and hip bone, and their likeness of members are saved according to the best conditions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not poisonous."
"And I will tell you what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may know how to treat them."
"That is why, in the case of a man who was pierced by an arrow in the lachrymal bone of his eye socket near the root of his nose, I extracted it from the other side leaving behind the gristle of his ear, and he recovered without any injury to his eye."
"And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which had struck him in the gristle of his eyelid, leaving behind the eyelid itself and the arrow was already hidden, and it was not possible to seize hold of it except for a little point by which it adhered to its shaft, and it was one of those sharp arrows of the Turks with a lion's head on which there were no feathers."
"And the Jew recovered, without any harm to his eye."
"And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat; it was an Arabian arrow with feathers on it. "And I extracted another arrow from a man who had been pierced in his belly and thought that he would die from it. And after thirty days had passed with no change in his condition due to his natural disposition, I made an incision over the arrow and extracted it, and he recovered without any harm."

"And I was called for his treatment as soon as three days had passed after he was struck by the arrow."

"Regarding venesection (flebotomia):"
"The veins usually opened during phlebotomy are: sixteen in the head, two pulsating veins near the ears, called temples; two veins apparent on both temples; two veins located at the corner of both eyes, called lacrimal veins; one vein prepared at the middle of the forehead and one at the tip of the nose; two veins at the neck; two veins at the upper lip; two veins at the lower lip; and finally, two veins behind the tongue." "The veins opened during phlebotomy in the arms are five: one is the cephalic vein which runs over the wild side (lateral side); it's called 'the head vein' and it's deaf; it's also a median vein composed of a branch from the basilica and another from the cephalic vein, which people call 'the vein of the whole body'; the basilica is the vein situated on the domestic (medial) side, known as 'cubital' or 'elbow vein' in common language; the funis of the arm runs over the elbow and is where phlebotomy takes place; it appears visibly on the wrist; the salvatella is the vein located between the auricular bone and the little finger's metacarpal, which divides into two branches."
"And in the thigh and leg there are three veins: one of them is located at the curvature of the kneecap on the lateral side (outside); the second is called saphenous, located near the bend of the inner thigh (medial side); and the third is the sciatic vein, located behind the outer thigh (lateral side). In the leg there are other veins similar to those mentioned."
"The benefit of opening veins near the ears is for ancient catarrhs, migraines, headaches and ulcers on old wounds."
"And the procedure for opening those veins is as follows: the patient should be lying down with their head tilted towards the occiput (back of the head) in a comfortable position. Then, wrap a cloth around the patient's head, making sure to cover both veins near the ears and the location between them on the forehead. Tighten the cloth gently but firmly, then feel for the pulse of the veins with your index finger. When you've found it, make the incision."
"Then, take the lancet, which is used for incising. Insert it into the vein in the elbow until blood flows out." "And elevate your hand with the vein and elbow upwards, then make an incision into the vein using an incising cut." "And make the length of the incision equal to two fingers' width or about that amount, then let as much blood flow out as desired."
"And afterwards apply powder and dress the wound until it heals."
"And as mentioned at the beginning of this book, these veins are also opened with cauterization."
"Among the two arterial veins on the temples, the benefit of phlebotomy is for ancient migraines, chronic bad humor, habitual inflammation of the eyes, and acute attacks of superfluous heat in the cheeks."
"And the procedure for phlebotomy of those veins is as follows: Have the patient lean their head forward until both veins appear clearly. Then feel for the pulse of the veins with your index finger, and once you've found it, make the incision."
"And elevate the elbow above the vein with your index finger. Insert the phlebotomy lancet into it, raise the vein up, and make an incision just as you did for the other two veins. Then let blood flow out according to your needs."
"And then untie the bandage from the patient's arm. Place your index finger on the vein for about an hour, then press a cotton cloth and powder onto the wound with strong pressure. Leave it like that until it heals."
"And as previously mentioned at the beginning of this book, these veins have been remembered and their incisions and attractions are described."

"And the procedure for phlebotomy of that vein is as follows: Have the patient lean their head back and place your left hand on their nose. And if you are provoked to evacuate blood again and there is no excuse, then do it on that same spot or on the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "the rope of the arm" is phlebotomized in place of the furca or basilica, when they are not visible or hidden, for it is composed of those.
And the quality of phlebotomy of that one is that the patient immerses his hand in cold water until the flesh reddens and the vein appears manifestly.
And make sure to tie it tightly with a ligature made of twisted thread with moderate tension; and phlebotomize according to the transverse direction, not latitudinally nor according to length.
And let the aperture be wide and your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize that one at the junction of the hand a little bit.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse your hand in cold water again and let the blood run into the water until your need is satisfied.
But if you are in the days of summer, then you are excused from the immersion of your hand in cold water; and in autumn the course of blood is stopped by immersing it in cold water at that time.
And phlebotomy from this vein is safer than from all other veins because it does not have a pulsating vein nor nerves.
And phlebotomy of the little finger of the right hand corresponds to diseases of the liver.
And the quality of phlebotomy from that one is this: you tighten the hand with a ligature or your hand, because you have placed it in cold water until the vein becomes inflamed and well demonstrated at sight; and make sure to phlebotomize it transversely; and if you miss it entirely, do not worry about it.
And be careful not to end the phlebotomy on your hand, because those ones have nerves denuded of flesh.
And make sure to immerse the hand in cold water again and let the blood run into that one because if you do not immerse it again in cold water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and its flow is prohibited.
And when the blood has been extracted according to the amount needed, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not close suddenly; and do likewise in all strict vein.
But be aware that phlebotomy of the little finger of the left hand corresponds to diseases of the spleen.
And do likewise in the phlebotomy of that one as you have done with the other.
And the advantage of phlebotomy from that vein is for diseases that are in the parts of the body, such as diseases of the mother, and retention of menstruation, etc. and also for ulcers of skin and old wounds, and their similar diseases.
And the quality of phlebotomy from that one is this: the patient immerses his foot in cold water and makes friction until the veins appear well; and he places it on the junction of the foot with a belt; and the place of the vein appears clearly below the said foot, and from there are many branches distributed on the face of the foot.
And then phlebotomize the largest branch or the one that is below the heel on the side of the aggregation of it because it is better and safer.
And if you phlebotomize that one on the face of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are below that one on the face of the foot, and pause your phlebotomy with which you phlebotomize transversely, as if you wanted to miss it.
And make sure that the phlebotomy is sufficient
But if the emission of blood is prohibited, then immerse the foot again in cold water and let the blood run into that one until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then repeat with the phlebotomy somewhat higher up because the place is safe; and do not fear its malignancy, but rather be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And do likewise with the saphenous vein of the other foot
And concerning the sciatic vein, its place is thus: below the heel on the side of the ankle of the left leg
And the advantage of phlebotomy from that one is for pain in the hip when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of phlebotomy from that one is this: the patient enters into a bath and makes haste and tightens the skin of that one on the side of the hip above the heel above four fingers with a long thin bandage because it does not appear except by that.
And then when it appears, then phlebotomize that one according to each vein that is within your ability and according to the transverse, and it is better; or miss that one by incision or cut that one by section because its place is safe.
And it is found more in men than women. And if you find that one, and it does not appear to the touch, then phlebotomize some branch of it, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot near the said ear and little finger bones.
Guard yourself against the nerves, and make the blood flow as much as you wish.
Then carefully untie the ligation and place your hand over the site of the phlebotomy wound, pressing gently to stop the bleeding. It heals easily this way.
Of ventilation and the quality of using those.
Ventilation is done with horns, or earms (earmuffs), or wicks
Ventilation is done in two ways: one with scarification and bloodletting, the other without scarification.
And this ventilation that is done without scarification is performed in two ways: either with fire or without fire
And the twelve locations of the body where ventilation is administered with scarification and bloodletting are: behind the ears; at the crown of the head; in the middle of both halves of the neck; below the jawbone on either side of the mouth; on the two shoulder blades; over the buttocks; on the inner thighs, each about a hand's breadth above the knee.
Ventilation does not draw blood from veins but from the small capillaries scattered throughout the flesh, and therefore does not drain the virtue through them, just as blood is drawn by phlebotomy. Neither does it matter whether ventilation is done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by excess within that needs to be evacuated completely from the body
But if there is a need for ventilation due to illness or continence, administer it every hour at the beginning and end of the month, and in the middle of it as well, whenever necessary.
Therefore, whenever any of those causes occur, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, we command that ventilation be performed after two or three hours have passed since daybreak.
It is helpful to ventilate the nape of the neck when there is heaviness in the head and sinking at the temples, but it is important that this be done as part of a general evacuation of the entire body.
This ventilation is performed on the same spot where phlebotomy is done on the cephalic vein.
It is not permitted to perform this procedure on anyone who is of robust constitution or has a cold, as it can cause great harm.
Therefore, this technique should not be used on the elderly or those with hot-headed illnesses.
But if one continues to perform this technique repeatedly on the same person, it can lead to forgetfulness. Therefore, you should command the operator performing ventilation to pause slightly with his hand and the cup at each of the intervals mentioned earlier, in order to prevent the generation of forgetfulness.
Ventilation of the armpit is done on the spot where phlebotomy is performed on the dark vein and the basilica vein: for this reason it relieves asthma, tightness in the chest, and splitting pain in the lungs, as well as coughing and congestion.
It is important that armpit ventilation be done with care, because if it is done too vigorously it can cause weakness in the body and stomach.
Ventilation of the forehead relieves accidental headaches and inflammation of the eyes, migraines, and congestion, as well as pain in the roots of the teeth. It is performed on the spot where phlebotomy is done on the basilica vein.
You should command the operator performing ventilation not to press his hand at the site of incision, lest he injure the artery and cause bleeding.
Ventilation of the chin relieves halitosis in the mouth, gingivitis, and symptoms of oral diseases. It is performed on the spot where phlebotomy is done on the veins found on both lips.
However, ventilation performed on the two spatulas (the sides of the neck) causes headache, which is caused by congestion of heat.
Ventilation of the two divisions at the corners of the eyes relieves symptoms associated with phlebotomy of three veins: the basilica, dark, and cephalic veins. This is because it attracts blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, as well as the smaller branches of the other larger veins that lead to those three veins.
Command the operator not to proceed with scarification at this location, as it is lacking in flesh and has nerves and arteries close beneath the surface.
This ventilation technique involves applying the cup over the buttocks and relieves hemorrhoids of the anus, as well as ulcers in that area.
You should command the operator to perform strong, broad ventilation at this location using a brass cup, as the area requires a strong section. Weak or subtle traction vents may not be sufficient and scarification may be necessary.
However, ventilation of the calves reduces pain manifestly by degrees, as it attracts blood from the entire body and relieves chronic pain in the loins and back, as well as the bladder. It also stimulates menstruation and relieves dropsy and edema. It is performed on the spot where phlebotomy is done on two saphenous veins, the external saphenous vein, and two cavel veins.
However, they greatly weaken the body and can cause fainting in many people.
The benefit of ventilation on the external saphenous vein is similar to that of ventilation on both calves.
The quality of the position for performing vents is to place the cup at the beginning and follow with moderate suction, not prolonging the position but pausing it lightly and removing it gently, allowing the humors to come to the location by equal attraction. Do not cease repeating this until you see the location become reddened and swollen, and you can perceive the color of the blood appearing within the cup.
Then scarify and repeat suction little by little, carefully considering the condition of the patient's body.
For a person with delicate skin and few pores, you should scarify once only and not more, so that the location is not ulcerated.
Command the operator to widen the scarification and probe slightly, and apply suction gently with ease.
If the blood is thick, you should then scarify twice, because in the first instance the pathway for suction of the blood and its humidity is opened. But in the second instance, the extraction of thick blood becomes more visible during examination.
If the blood is very fetid, then perform a third scarification to ensure its complete removal.
Finally, when you wish to remove blood little by little or just a small amount, one scarification is sufficient.
If you wish to remove a large amount of blood, perform many scarifications. If you know that the blood is thick, then you should scarify with a deep cut.
The term "scarification" refers solely to the depth of the incision made by a lancet.

If it's summertime, use oil of aloes or violet oil, or bitter almond oil, or oil from cooked hemp; but if it's wintertime, then use poppy oil, lilac oil, chamomile oil, or blood."

Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On restoration This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine, as it concerns the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Oh, son, know that those who boast about this chapter are foolish doctors and laymen who at no time have regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter of them.
And for this reason, the science of this manner in our land is rare. And I have not forgotten it at any time. And I do not propose what follows except due to the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying their understanding, in order to extract the science from them.
And therefore I direct you to experiment and study throughout the duration of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and about which my experiment has passed. For here I have summarized it for you other wise men and delivered it from the occupation of lengthening; and I have abbreviated it in abbreviation; and I have shown it in declaration; and I have formed many forms of the instruments that are administered therein, as is done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two preceding chapters.
Chapter one
Summary and collections of the causes of bone fractures, from which it is necessary to make a premise before we begin remembering the members that have been broken and diverse. I therefore wish to declare in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of my speech, and the chapters for understanding which at the beginning it is necessary for craftsmen to stand still and reflect on their truth. So you are and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art besides you.
And therefore I say that when some fracture or dislocation or torsion or collapse occurs, at the beginning it is necessary for you to prepare with a phlebotomy or bleed that one, or several together, if no prohibition exists due to debility of virtue or because he was an infant, or elderly in decrepitude, or during intense heat or cold.
And therefore you should be content with your diet of fresh herbs and meats of birds and crabs. And prohibit thick meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, lest you are secure about an abscess forming and fear effusion of matter in the member.
And at this point return the sick to his first regimen with which he is accustomed to live.
And therefore when the broken bone begins to be restored, at this point the sick should be nourished with foods rich in nutrients that are thick and heavy, containing viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, rice, chickpeas, eggs, beef, cheese, fresh fish, thick wine, and similar things. And with this regimen coagulation of the fracture is done more easily and quickly.
And know that when bones are broken in men who are in the first flourishing of life or are elderly, it is not possible for them to be joined together and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of the members.
But nevertheless nature adjusts and binds the broken bone together with glue-like substance that adheres to it, so that they are joined together at the same time. And it brings about the end of firmness and strength, just as fire does at its beginning. As long as no cause prohibits this from occurring in its workings.
And for this reason it is necessary to pause the feeding of the sick with foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members. For a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm, and similarly all other members have different fractures among themselves.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinguished from the others.
Many varieties of fracture even within itself. And the types of bone fractures are diversified within themselves; for there is an arch-like fracture that occurs sometimes in stars. And some fractures occur according to the length of the bone: such as those of the satire and astragalus bones which are additional or not, just like when a pen is prepared to write. And there is also a fracture with splintering and disruption at the head, and a fracture with a small fissure.
And for each type there is a specific pain or torment in its restoration, which will be remembered later on distinguished by their locations.
Many signs of bone fracture And a fracture of the bone is known by its crookedness, and by the prominence and depression that can be felt on it, and also when you squeeze it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent crookedness in the place nor any deformity felt when touched with agitation and the sick person does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture here.
It may happen that there is slight crookedness or fissure present, so you should not squeeze it with your hand or compress it too much. Rather pause at this point and use the medicines of which a reminder will be given at this moment suitable for the location; and also apply a bandage to provide subtle support.
And know that when the location is arched and not split into two or otherwise injured in its satire, if all separation from its companion is removed, then you should begin at this point to correct it, provided that an abscess does not occur there.
And if an abscess occurs there, then leave it alone for several days until the abscess stops. And then treat it according to each manner that you know of lifting and binding.
And know that restoration and healing of this is easier than for the bone in which satire occurs.
And then strengthen it as will be remembered later on.
But if there are satiruses in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two parts if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two strings or one string and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the affected member in its natural figure while the parted parts of the broken member are extended; then you should proceed to return those additions with binding and according to each manner that you can apply them in their locations.
"Guard thou thyself, according to thy strength, that thou fall not upon the patient through thine own carelessness or injury. And proceed thou with all thy might, applying one bone with another their companionship in their best forms."
"And it is necessary that thou dost touch him at that opportune moment with thy hand, and if sometimes some different cause occurs, correct and equalize it according to the quantity of thy virtue."
"And excessive extension and compression are avoided, as many foolish doctors do who often cause an abscess to be cut by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen from their operations."
"And this is the quality of bandaging that I recommend for tranquil limbs, because you should know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and greatness and form."
"And for those that are small, such as the arm, hand, and foot, and similar to them, then the wrapping should be of very fine linen."
"But for those that are large, such as the thigh or calf, then in these there should be wrapping of thick and strong linen, because the tight binding connects the limb and strengthens it on all sides with equal bandaging, so that nothing enters between them."
"And when you begin, first direct your evacuation of air, then bind it conveniently with three or four wrappings according to the size of that limb, and tighten thy hand with the linen."
"And then take him to the highest part of that limb from the location of the fracture, and tighten more so that thy bandage is not too loose at the place of the fracture." "And then extend yourself with wrapping from the location of the fracture a little bit, and gradually loosen the bandage, until you take up a large part of it."
"And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the location of the broken limb with wrapping, and then go with those wrappings to the highest part of that limb away from the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the wrapping and loosening of that according to what we have said about the first wrapping above."
"And then insert between the wrappings of linen or cloth with which you should equalize any curvature of the fracture, if there is curvature in it; and if not, do not pause therein for any cause."
"And then place another bandage over it, and then equalize upon those wrappings with well-arranged stitches immediately, if there is no inflammation or abscess in that location, and then place over that which sits on the abscess and covers the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then tighten over it the stitches."
"And let the stitches be of strong linen thread, well-formed; or of juniper wood; or of date palm branches; and let the width of each stitch be according to this shape and figure:"
"But the stitch that is placed upon that fracture should be thicker and stronger than the other stitches, and the length of the stitch according to what is measured on the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the stitches with another bandage according to thy first tightening; and then tie it above with thread neatly according to what we have said about tightening, and let thy tightening be according to the location of the fracture more, and as it is further away from the fracture, so less should be the tightening."
"And let the threads be soft in thickness and fineness, and of wool; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some fools doing who place their threads of twisted cords, it is a great error, for they will not have an even tightening."
"And very fine threads are not convenient, for with them you will not be able to tighten as much as you want, and it is necessary that there should be less than one digit between stitch and stitch."
"And if the pain is felt at the ends of the stitches near the tightening in those locations, then place them aside with linen or wool pads until that does not cause harm."
"Sometimes fracture is with splinters, and there occurs displacement in the bone, and I have spoken about that previously in this chapter concerning singularities."
"And know that not every broken limb should be stitched with stitches on the first day; which is because when a limb is large, it is necessary to wait upon it with stitches for about five or seven or more days, according to your security concerning the advent of an abscess."

If it is scraped, then comfort it with water containing humid coriander or simply with water.
Another method for a plaster that is more powerful in resolution than the first one, when an apostema occurs during the restoration of a limb: take some roots of althea and its seeds, and of balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, and all this finely chopped and sifted; then grind them together with water from sauces or sweet water or wine, all according to the quality of the limb and the pause of its color.
However, Hippocrates does not specify in his book that it should be paused on the limb for forty days during its restoration, but rather recommends making a cerote of wax and oil, and nothing else; he also advises that it should be ground between coarseness and subtlety.
But it is seen that in Galen's view, they should be paused on the broken limb during its restoration and the causes for which there is drying with little heat are such as myrrh, resins, and similar ones of those causes that we have recommended.
A way to make a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take some myrrh, saffron, human hair cuttings or chicken feathers, althea, and salt, all finely chopped; grind them together until they form a plaster.
However, the amount to be left in place according to how tightly it should remain and when it is appropriate to remove it depends on whether there is no pain or swelling nor any bone fracture, in which case you can leave it for several days. And if there is severe pain or swelling or inflammation in the place where it has been applied, then you should immediately remove it and replace the plaster.
Then take some lint and marine sponge moistened with hot water, and submerge it in warm water; use this to wash the affected area until the swelling subsides and the pain is relieved. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour. After that, place lanolin soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil on the limb and leave it overnight, ensuring that there is no signs of cauterization, and that the swelling and pain have been removed from the limb.
And then apply that subtle strapping and small plaster again, not too tightly as in your first application, until it is healed.
And if sometimes the abscess and redness and pain and inflammation and all accidents are completely removed, and you need a plaster and strapping, then repeat that which you have done at the beginning of treatment.
And if no cause of those mentioned occurs in the limb, then you should not remove it but leave it on for three or four or five or seven days. And sometimes all this is left until twenty days according to what appears to you when removing the plaster, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then tighten the strapping more than your first strapping all around, and adjust it according to the thickness of the limb of the sick person, according to how much it has healed.
But if the place of the fracture becomes dry and emaciated more than it should, then you should know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching that place. Therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you remove the plaster; do this for all three days. And loosen the strapping a little, and through this operation, bring nourishment to the limb, and it will be healed easily.
But what foolish bone-setters do when a fracture of the limb is not restored at the beginning, as it should be, and is restored crookedly, that is an error according to their operation.
