"I have never seen them do this, so it seems more reasonable that they should not."
"Chapter Two: On fracture when it occurs in the head."
Broken bones are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by impact of force which crushes all of the bone within its cavity, leaving the bone like that seen in a tree trunk, and hence this type is called "axial"; or the force cuts into some part of the bone, cutting through the flesh but not penetrating to the marrow; and this kind of fracture is called "closed".
The disease of these two fractures is great or small, and it varies from fracture to fracture. The cause of this is impact with force or pressure on bone, accompanied by swelling or bruising.
This fracture is penetrating and approaches the perimysium which covers the bone, or it occurs on the surface of the bone. The severity of these two fractures varies greatly.
And there is fracture which is concentrated within the bone itself, in the finest detail of hair's breadth, and hence this type of fracture is called "capillary".
And there is fracture which occurs by pressure or impact of bone, with similar symptoms, causing some laminae of the bone to enter into its cavities, and this happens especially in concave areas, as seen in the heads of hammers when they suffer impact and their entrance is fractured.
This phenomenon occurs most often in the bodies of moist bones, such as the heads of nails.
And so for all these types of fractures, they appear like stars separated from one another. "I will bring all of this back to its proper place."
"I know all these types of fracture by detection on those and by inquiry of their marrow and examination of putrid flesh that is upon them."
"A capillary fracture is recognized by the fact that it is visible on the bone and is cleaned and lined with tincture of iodine because the fracture appears black."
"However, in the treatment of fractures, one must first consider any accidents of the illness, and then whether the accidents are significant by manifest sign, such as vomiting from cholera or extension and loss of consciousness, fainting spells, acute fever, dilation of pupils and redness of eyes, and similar occurrences in major conditions; it is not healed if not treated."
"And if there are no current accidents you wait for health, then you begin the treatment of it."
"The reason for this is that if it happens to you at the beginning as it was happening and especially in the days of winter, then it is necessary for you to observe the marrow of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all conditions."
"And if it is in the days of summer, then you must expose the bone before the seventh day, having ensured that what remains of the bone has become corrupted by the soft tissue, and that the accidents which we have mentioned have occurred."
"And if the fracture has already reached the soft tissue covering the brain and is accompanied by small pieces and attrition, then you must insert the comminuted and attrition fragments, according to what I will recommend to you."
"And having shaved the head of the patient, you must uncover the bone according to your custom and manner as much as possible following the figure of the fracture, ensuring that it is light on the patient."
"And if it happens to you that you must make the detection of the bone, fluxes of blood or hard abscess, then you should pause for it with what is necessary, and this is that you apply compresses of cloth infused in wine and rose oil to the place, and let the fracture rest until the abscess is sedated, and ensure there are no fluxes of blood; and then begin the circumcision of the bone and its ablation."
"And this is according to one or two methods of operation."
"And one method is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision, and this is its shape: And then administer another incision tool a little wider than the first one; this is its shape:"
"And then apply an even wider incision tool."
"And you should have a variety of different incision tools, some of which are wider than others; some shorter than others and sharp at their tips, made of iron or steel."
"And use them with ease in percussion on the incision tool so that you do not strike the head and harm it."
"And if the bone is strong and hard, then you must perforate around it before you administer the incision tools with their handles, which are called non-penetrating awls."
"And they are not called thus but rather those which do not reach the end of the bone at that point where it is approaching it, so that the awl has a round tip or edge on that side which is left of its sharp tip similar to an edge or small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone."
"And it is convenient that you take many of these awls, each suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have present in all tests of the brain the tip of which is sharp, and its quantity in length and width according to the quantity of thickness of this test."
"And this is the shape of three types of awls: The quality of perforating around the broken bone is that you place the awl on the bone and twist it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated; and then move the awl to another location, and pause where it is between all threads equal to the thickness of the ray or approximately."
"And then incise with incision tools between each thread of the bone and make it as subtle as possible in the end of levitation, as we have said, until you remove the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause by some instruments which will be prepared for that, such as tweezers and subtle forceps."
"And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the incision tool does not touch any part of the cerebrum."
"And then when you have completely perforated the bone and separated it from the cerebrum if it is continuous with it, you will extract it, and rasp and smooth it on its roughness where it is on the bone with another tool similar to an incision tool but which must be more thin and subtle than the others."
"And if some fragments of the small bones remain scattered and scattered, then you should take them with ease with what is necessary among the tools; and then cure the wound with stitches and bandages which we have mentioned."
"And the other method of operation is light and distant from fear, which Gaulish and Greek praise greatly; this is its speech: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin discovering the fracture of the bone in that place where it is most violent and evident, until when you have discovered that fracture and placed on one side of it the tip of this long and gentle incision tool whose shape is as follows:"
"And it is that the lenticular fracture is lion-shaped and does not incise any part, and the acute fracture is of it on both sides in length, as often happens; so that the lenticular fracture fits well with the cerebrum with its face facing the sharp edge of the bone."
"And then strike the incision tool of one fracture with a small hammer until you incise all the bone easily as it revolves and you are sure that the panic does not happen in any manner whatsoever, even if the patient were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep."
"Then if some part of the paniculus attached to the bone in certain places of bone breaks off, incise it with the tip of this lenticular incision tool and detach it easily because it is detached from it without harm or fear."
"And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the paniculus and that which is cut off or detached from the bone is its face, and remains rough and scattered with subtle fragments then it is necessary to rasp this roughness and remove these fragments with subtle razors which you have prepared in different quantities so that you can administer them in all places as conveniently and correctly as possible according to how you proceed in your operation of that broken bone and the shape of it."
"And it is necessary that you use in your operation with which you rasp the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors and then use progressively the most subtle ones, and do not cease doing so continuously until you arrive at administering the thinnest and most subtle of all."
"But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, administer cure according to what you have found for their correction."
"And it is a cause that it is not mentioned among all those who have studied in this art." "And what we have written about this applies especially to large fractures."
"And if the paniculus is torn when you remove the bone, then it is necessary to take a piece of cloth according to the quantity of the wound and immerse it in rose oil and place it on the wound; and also take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it on the first cloth gently so that you do not press too hard, and then apply this bandage and do not stretch it except according to the quantity that the wound has alone."
"Let that one remain under this care for one or two days until you are sure of the formation of pus; then remove it and administer medicines with which superfluous dryness is removed, such as linseed oil and barley flour, powder of incense and aloes, and similar things."
"Also make powders from these medicines which are finely powdered on the wound, like they are dry; generally, administer to him all medicine of a property that cleanses and does not bite."
"And guard yourself, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean and not sullied by dirt or other things; do not allow it to become inflamed, because when it adheres to the panniculus of the brain it corrupts it and causes severe illness."
"When it occurs in the head, if the bone is exposed and especially if the treatment of it is neglected, then blackness appears on its surface; therefore consider the hours so that you know if it happens to the patient those accidents which we have said are fatal without any remedy."
"But if blackness does not appear except from the medicine applied to it and that blackness is due to the power of that medicine, then take some honey and three parts rose oil, mix them with a cloth, and apply it directly on the wound; also treat it with the appropriate remedies until it is healed."
"Chapter III."
"On the restoration of the nose when it is cut."
"You should know that the nose is not broken except into two parts at once raised up, or one of those sometimes seen, because there are two bones in it, and the cartilage part is not broken but only roughened and twisted."
"But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should introduce the little finger into its place and push that fracture inward with your thumb and index finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape." "And let this be done by you easily, and guard yourself so that you do not cause pain to the patient during your operation."
If the fracture is at the top of the nose where it meets the forehead, and the patient cannot reach that area, then a splint should be applied with its end placed on the bone in which some thickness has been carved.
If the fracture is in two parts, then make it appear as if it were one piece.
And try to begin the restoration of it on the first day after the fracture if possible; otherwise, around the seventh or tenth day you should repeat the wound cleansing process.
And if the fracture is in one piece, insert a cloth wick into the end of the nose, or use two cotton pads if it's in two pieces, ensuring that the padding is thick enough to fill the gap in the nose.
Some ancient restorers say that the cotton pads should be edged with border and changed every day, but this is not seen by me. It is necessary that the wick be wrapped in albumen of egg yolk mixed with powdered mastic; and also let the pad remain inside until the bone has been set and the cartilage strengthened.
Sometimes, instead of cotton pads, pieces of linen wool can be inserted into the nose, provided that there is not much pus present and that the patient's breathing is not impeded.
And this method is not necessary for you to use; if you wish, do as I have said, or if you prefer, use cotton pads.
And if another wound cleansing process is needed in the future, plaster the nose with cerote or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if there is no wound cleansing process needed, then you should plaster the outside of the nose with sesame flour and powdered mastic mixed with egg white. And also cover it with linen wool, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or slightly comminuted, then you should bind them up and extract those fragments using appropriate instruments. Then close the wound with stitches and treat it with something that heals and consolidates the bone, along with suitable plasters.
If plague occurs within the nostrils, then it is necessary to cure them with hot compresses and administer lead cannulae until completely healed.
Captulo IV
De la restauracin de la mandbula cuando est trabada
When the jawbone is broken and the fracture is not infected, look to see if it is fractured only on one side, and not in two pieces. If it is curved inward at the ends, its treatment will be easier
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth and similarly if the fracture is on the left jawbone, insert the said index finger of the right hand. Then gently push the swelling of the fracture inward with that finger while supporting the outside of the bone with your other hand until it becomes even.
And if the jawbone fracture is already separated into two pieces, then you must apply traction to both parts according to their straightness until it is possible for them to be brought together.
And if there is commotion of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those which you expect to break with gold or silver wire or thread. Then place cotton on the broken jawbone and apply gauze over it, and place a large sterile cloth folded lengthwise on top of everything. Then bind it all together according to how you are accustomed to do so and according to what is convenient for its application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to rest quietly, and have them avoid eating dry foods that are difficult to swallow.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in some way, then on the third day remove the splint and realign it. Then refine what needs to be refined on the splint and apply plaster with powdered chalk or egg albumin or wheat flour, after first removing the cotton from the patient's mouth. Then place a linen cloth over the plaster.
And then, when the splint is attached and well-adjusted on the patient, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it in place until the fracture has healed and become strong. For many times this fracture heals in three weeks.
And if an abscess forms later on, then apply what we have said many times for its treatment until it heals and the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is accompanied by pus, then consider whether it is a piece of bone that has separated or an abscess. If it is an abscess, then be careful to remove those abscesses using whatever is convenient for removing them with your instruments.
"If the orifice of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it using a dilator according to your need."
"And when you have finished those procedures and nothing remains, then if the wound's orifice is wide; otherwise, apply one of the dressings that are convenient for this purpose and pack it until it heals."
"Chapter Five."
"On the restoration of the collarbone when broken."
"Frequently, the collarbone is broken at its midpoint near the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways."
"It is broken, separated, or separated into two pieces without any displacement; this is easy to restore; or it occurs with displacements, which makes restoration difficult; or it is accompanied by a wound."
"And the operation in this case, when there is no wound, is that you have two assistants present: one to hold the forceps and follow the broken collarbone, and the other to extend the neck at its midpoint."
"And once you have grasped the fracture with your hands as described earlier, make sure it has the appropriate shape according to what is needed, and that there is neither prominence nor concavity at its apex."
"And if you need more extension, then place a folded cloth or wool pad behind the sick person's shoulder according to how much is necessary for your needs. Then extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand while grasping the fracture as needed."
"And if you cannot reduce the end of the collarbone to its normal position from outside, then have the patient sit up on their neck and place a large cushion or bolster under their shoulder on that side."
"And the assistant presses down on the muscle at the fracture site while you elevate the collarbone until it is reduced to its normal position. Then you can correct the fracture and grasp it with your hands as described earlier."
"But if there are displacements of the collarbone that cause pain and discomfort, then you should press down on them and remove those displacements with ease."
"And if it is retained within the bone, then you should make an incision through it using one of the incissors that you have prepared for this purpose. You had previously placed the collarbone under the instrument called a 'cyphac,' which can be made of wood or iron and has this shape:"
"And it resembles a spoon with a concave surface, whose width should be according to your needs based on the size of the bone or the depth of the incision."
"But its length should be according to what is feasible for the operation: it should have two ends like this, one wide and the other narrow."
"And if the disruption of the skin that you incised when removing the bone displacement is wide and you are certain there will be an abscess, then you should approximate the two edges of the wound with sutures."
"And if the disruption is small or you fear an abscess, then fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the size of the skin incision."
"If an abscess forms, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil mixed with wine, and place it on that area."
"And all this should be done without causing pain to the patient over the fractured bone. And you should check on the patient every day to see if the bandage becomes loose or slips out of place; if so, then correct it and tighten the ligature."
"And also place the patient on their back and elevate their head with pillows, placing one pillow on either side of them at night. This way, the assistant's arm is elevated from that side and consequently the fractured collarbone is elevated due to the elevation of the muscle; or else the patient's arm is tied around their neck."
"And do not remove the bandage until the twelfth day unless there is proper healing or an abscess occurs."
"And also renew the poultice if you see it become dry at any time, adjusting it to fit the reduction and replacing it until the fracture is completely restored and consolidated."
"And it is strengthened and comfortable after one month and twenty-eight days. Sometimes in some men it happens more quickly than this."
"Chapter VI."
"On the restoration of fractures of the shoulder."
"The blade of the scapula is broken at its lateral edge and not the whole bone, but only its margins."
"And therefore when the fracture is at its lateral edge and not known except by touch, then prepare it according to its shape and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that you are able."
"And also apply a poultice of fine powdered millstone with egg albumen and a lion's tooth compress. Then place a folded cloth bandage over it; then apply a thin wooden splint according to the size of the entire blade or slightly larger."
"But if there is a concavity on one side of the blade where the splint lies, then fill in that concavity with a lion's tooth compress and place the splint evenly."
"And also secure it above with a long twisted bandage with sufficient tension and firmness of the splint so that it does not slip out of its place."
"And check the bandage every day. And whenever it becomes loose, replace it and tighten it again, adjusting the splint if it has slipped out of its place."
"And make the patient lie on their side."
"The blade is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five days."
"And therefore when this number is complete, then dissolve the bandage, ensuring that you know that it is among those bones where healing has begun and its removal will not cause harm."
"And if you proceed with the operation at the location of the shoulder blade and put the arm in place, then cut it open there, drain it, and follow what we have said concerning the treatment of an abscess if one occurs here."
"Chapter VII."
"On the restoration of fractures of the thighbone."
"The thighbone sometimes breaks in its middle but most often it is broken at both ends and becomes shortened and comminuted."
"And when the middle of it is broken, its accidents are that it declines towards the thighs and occurs with great pain, difficulty in walking, coughing, shortness of breath, and vomiting blood. It is evident in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden to sensation."
"And the restoration of him is that the patient should be propped up on their back, and a cushion placed between their shoulders; then press upon their muscles, adjust their ribs with both hands, and proceed gently in the operation and equitation according to whatever manner is possible for this purpose."
"And then place the plaster and cotton wool on top of a subtle table made of wood or marble, and its semblance in levitation, since you wrap it in cloth. And be careful to apply that gently onto the broken bone without hurting him; and bind around the back several times according to the roundness of the spine, tightening it appropriately. Observe the binding at all times, and when softened, tighten it again."
"And if it is necessary to provoke the removal of that binding due to infection or pain, or an aposteme occurring in that location, then you gently remove and untie that binding, eradicate the plaster, and rectify whatever has happened there through appropriate care. And then reduce the plaster, sometimes applying it to the face; and adjust the tightness until it is healed."
"Chapter VIII."
"On the restoration of crushed ribs."
"You should know that rib fractures only occur in large, thick bones of the back; and at their extremities near the junction, such damage does not happen except due to a fall." "And its diagnosis is not hidden from sensation during the examination of it with these tools."
"And the restoration should be done as follows: align the fracture with these tools according to the most possible manner, until it is completely aligned according to need, and then apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if necessary."
"And if the rib fracture is displaced towards its ends inward, then it occurs with sudden pain and stabbing, like that which occurs in someone who has coughed up blood, because the bone pierces the pleura; and difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood occur, and this is difficult to treat."
"And even the Ancients knew about this with many words."
"And they said this: that one should pause feeding the patient who generates inflation and flatulence, so that the abdomen may become inflated and extended, and thus the fracture may be pushed back towards its ends outward."
"But we would avoid this because it might cause a boil to form easily if it does not happen, and if it has happened, then it will already help with that."
"And some of them said that wind should be paused and sucked out with force; this is similar reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the wind might draw the superfuities to the location due to weakness."
"And some of them said: that the location should be covered with wool which has already been immersed in hot oil, and powdered resin should be paused between the ribs until it is full, so that the wrapping may fit well according to its roundness; and then the patient should be treated with food and medicine for pleurisy."
"And if the patient is experiencing strong pain which they cannot bear, and the bone pierces the pleura with sharp puncture, then we fear for the patient, so it is necessary to perform a operation on that location, uncovering the broken rib, and then place upon it the instrument that preserves the cicatrix, of which the memory has been preceded, and gently insert the bone and remove it."
"And then we should join together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with suturing, and treat it with plasters until it has healed."
"And if an abscess forms inside it, then take and grind up frankincense in rose oil and pause it upon the location, and treat the patient with what is necessary for that internal abscess."
"And let him be propped up on pillows on the side on which he lies, until he has healed."
Chapter IX
On the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck.
Sometimes fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it tends to adhere to them, and similarly for the spines of the back. "And when this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider: if the muscles around it are already softened, stunned, dead, and cannot move them nor extend nor strengthen them, and when you press on them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they do not feel pain in that location, then you will know that it has not healed, and such cases are mortal."
"But if they can move them and feel pressure with the fingers or pricking, then you will know that the neck of the bone is saved, and the patient will recover with treatment."
"But if it appears like this on the spines of the neck, then consider its weight to know whether it will heal or not."
"Then if you find that the muscles around it are already softened, as happened with the others mentioned earlier, and furthermore when he lies on his back, there is windiness and emptying without his will, and sometimes he is propped up on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you will know that it is mortal, and therefore do not bother with treating him."
"But if none of these causes occur, then the case is better and easier."
"And the treatment for it is that you should pierce and evacuate the abscess which has formed on the broken spine with hot rose oil or with hot wax, pausing upon it a few times each day until the hard abscess has been softened."
"And then pause one of the comforting and exciting plasters on it, and strengthen it with bandages; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and not to lie down on the part where they feel pain until they have healed."
"And if it happens that the bone is sticking out or some cause has already separated from it, then it is necessary to secure a splint on the neck and fix that bone in place; and also join together the edges of the wound with suturing if it is large; and also treat it with some encrusting of the ointments until it has healed."
"But if the end of the bone has broken off, and it is the end of the spine, then you must insert your left-hand thumb into the anus, and grasp the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible by equilateral pulling; and also pause upon it plaster and bandage, if necessary for those, and also strengthen it."
"And if you feel broken bone fragments in it, then remove those and fix that bone in place, treating the wound with the treatment that has been described until it has healed."
Chapter X.
"On restoring health."
"The bones of the thighs are broken here, and if they break, the fracture is merely union at the ends of those bones which are inserted in length and incline toward the inside; and it happens that the patient suffers pain in this place and puncture, and the limb becomes paralyzed when there is a fracture."
"And the restoration of him consists in your extending your hand over that part which is above the fracture as figured."
"And if the fracture is only in the extremity of it then you shall reduce that according to what appears equal to you by proportion; and engage that part which is joined together with its natural figure."
"But if the fracture is in the long bone or already inclines toward the inside of the leg then let the patient lie on his back with that limb prepared for you to restore that fracture." "And then when you have reduced it, apply plaster, and also apply a splint of wood or stick, and bind it firmly with bandages so that there is no fear of puncture or displacement of the bone."
"And then fill in the concavity with something that will occupy that space which has been filled by the bandages according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side."
"But if an aposteme occurs, cease from the extension and restoration of it until the aposteme is sedated, and apply what sedates that according to what has been said before; and then return to the restoration of it, and bind it as usual."
"And if any splinters or small fragments occur in the bone, then remove them."
Chapter XI
Of the restoration of the fracture of the jawbone.
The jawbone is that which is between the cheek and the head of the shoulder.
"If it is broken, then its restoration is by one of two methods, the first being that you should take a splint of moderate thickness in this manner:"
"And if there are two extremities to it, two bands should be suspended from an elevated place nearby."
"Then have the patient sit on a chair, and carefully place their broken arm on the splint so that it reaches the elbow joint at the midpoint where the curvature of the splint is attached."
"Then suspend some heavy weight from that (the splint), or have the servant extend it towards the left side; and then the physician should gently pull the broken ends together with his hands, ensuring that the fracture is realigned properly."
"The other method is for the patient to be propped up on their chest, with their arm suspended by a sling from the neck. Then command two ministers: one should hold what is above the fracture with both hands, and the other should hold what is below it. And each of them should extend towards his side."
"And if you wish for greater extension, then place one strap above the fracture and another below it, and each of the two ministers should pull towards their respective sides."
"And if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then you should place half of the strap on the elbow joint and the other half below the fracture at the bone."
"And likewise, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then you should place the strap on that location and also over the elbow."
"Then apply it with ease and without difficulty, and as soon as it is applied correctly and according to what is necessary, then tie it firmly so that it does not happen that a blister forms."
"But if a blister forms, then loosen the bandage for 7 days, and apply over it a poultice made of linseed and oil, until the blister disappears. Then tie on again."
"And the method of tying that is: apply a plaster over the fracture, then wrap it with a layer of clean cloth over the plaster. Then adjust the arm over that support and place the hand covered in a sling on top of its muscle."
"And wrap and tie the cloth over the support for the arm so that the arm remains in place if there is no prohibition due to any cause of the broken bone."
"And then apply splints."
"And it is necessary to place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the others. And place the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on either side."
"And then tie over the splints with the bandage which was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And it is necessary that your bandage over the fracture site be as tight as possible." "And when the fracture is extended, the bandage should be looser."
"And therefore, if you adjust the splints and bandage as we have said in the time when you restore the member to its proper position, then do it."
"And if you fear blistering, then loosen the bandage and splints for 7 days as we have said."
"And then consider the bandage in all cases for 3 days so that no bruising or inflammation occurs at the location due to excess tightness. Or if the member is prohibited from being nourished due to excess tightness, then correct everything as we have said."
"But if you are doubtful about this and fear that nothing might happen due to it, then do not loosen the bandage, but rather wait for 3 days or more."
"And let his sleeping place be elevated on two pillows, and let his hands be on his stomach."
"And place over the elbow joint a full cushion of wool padding, and watch at all times of the night or day that the figure of the broken member is not dislocated nor dissolved, or if the bandage becomes softened, then correct everything with your skill."
"And feed the patient according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, so that in the beginning the food is soft until he begins to heal. And then it becomes necessary to increase his food."
"Regarding the custom of the splint and the sling, they should remain tied for 40 days. And then it becomes necessary to loosen them and give a bath and apply ointments that correct them."
"And if the fracture is completely consolidated, then you do not loosen the bandage and splints for 1 day or up to two months."
"Chapter XII."
"Of the restoration of fractures of the arm."
"The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two elbow bones. Of these, one is small and it secures the thumb; and the other is large and it is placed beside the little finger from its outer side."
"And sometimes only the larger elbow bone is fractured, or sometimes both are broken together."
"When the little elbow bone above is fractured, then its restoration will be easier and healing will also be quicker."
"But when it is fractured below, its fracture is bad and healing is difficult."
"And the worst of all is when both bones are broken together."
"Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the little elbow bone above, then it is convenient for the physician to do its restoration by applying a small extension with ease until it becomes well aligned."
"But if the large elbow bone is broken, then it is necessary to apply stronger traction."
"And if both bones are fractured together, then you must apply traction very strongly."
"And it is necessary that you place the figure of the hand in its restoration and traction extended on a pillow. And let the wrist be placed above all the fingers, with the thumb higher than all the other fingers, and let the patient lie on a cushioned bed."
"And place a pillow before him at his height or behind him, so that the patient does not move."
"And then let one assistant extend the arm from its outer side with his hand or bandage, and another assistant extend it similarly above."
"Then the physician aligns the bone until he reduces it to its best figure possible."
"And if in the fracture of the bone you are unsuccessful, then proceed to reduce all pressure into its proper place with your skill."
"And if it appears that the elbow is dislocated and does not return to its proper place during restoration, then do as we have said above."
"But if the fracture is accompanied by contusion, which has already been discussed in Chapter X, and therefore take care of it accordingly."
"And if blistering appears at the beginning of your restoration, then take a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness. Then apply the cloth over it with gentle pressure until the blister is absorbed. Then remove the cerate and apply an emplaster prepared from powder of barilla with clay of eggs. And then apply bandages; and let the bandage which is applied over that fracture be wide and strong."
"And know that in the case of fractures of the arm, the number of bandages used is usually greater than those used for other parts of the body, whether it be a fracture of one bone or both bones together."
"And then apply your bandage over the location of the fracture with great force and vehemence. And if the traction pulls too much on the parts above or below the fracture, then apply the bandage more gently according to what is remembered from the beginning of this chapter."
"Wet bandages should be wrapped around the fracture, not hard or rough ones. The thread with which it is sewn should be neither too thin nor too thick."
"The limb and its ligaments should be considered several days later." "And if some cause occurs that necessitates correction, such as swelling of the limb, then you should immerse the limb in cold water until the swelling subsides."
"Allow the limb to rest without constraint and then gradually return it to its original position."
"And if the dislocation has already been softened and the bone has returned to its usual appearance, then correct everything with your own strength."
"Also consider whether the supply of nutrients to the limb is being restricted by excessive tightness. If so, you should loosen it slightly and allow it to rest for a few days until blood flow improves, and then bind it again."
"But if the injury does not involve any of the causes mentioned above, then it should not be unbound for at least 20 days or thereabouts."
"And then have the injured person use their own strength to move their arm actively, and make them sleep on their back."
"And know that this fracture takes about 30 to 32 days to heal, or sometimes even fewer, such as 28 days, depending on the disposition of the complexion and the strength of the person."
"Chapter 13"
"On the restoration of fractures of the hand and fingers."
Know that at the tip of the hand and the knuckles of those fingers fracture occurs frequently, yet it rarely happens without first experiencing many dislocations.
And when the hand suffers fracture or dislocation, it is necessary that the patient lie on their back with the injured arm extended out in front of them and supported by a pillow.
And then the minister should extend the broken bones and the physician should bind them together with good alignment.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless there is a risk of pus formation. Then apply a splint according to the severity of the injury, which you should already have wrapped in cloth.
But if the fracture is in the middle of the palm, then fashion a splint like a hand towel and instruct the patient to grasp it with their injured hand. Then bind it with a long strip of cloth.
And the splint should be made of leather covered in cloth that is folded over itself to provide padding, so that the splint can be applied directly to the palm. Then bind it according to what is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside of the hand, then you should apply splints both above and below the injury on the palm side of the hand, so that the hand is in a position of flexion. Then bind it as follows:
And if there is a fracture of one of the bones in the fingers, then if it is the thumb, splint it as necessary and bind it to the palm. Additionally, you may apply a small splint on the outside of the finger to elevate the fracture and prevent displacement.
But if the fracture is in one of the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then splint it by extending and binding it with the finger next to it that is not injured. Alternatively, you may bind them all together in continuity, which is preferable. Or apply a small splint on top of the injured finger, as previously described for the thumb.
And note that in your treatment and splinting, you should consider the position of any blisters or wounds formed during the procedure, as well as what is necessary when such causes arise according to what is described in this book.
