Chapter XIV
"The bone of the hip joint is often broken, and this is felt according to its transformation into the anterior part and upper part."
"And its restoration is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament on the other side of the fracture, and the patient should lie prone on their stomach."
"And therefore the minister should extend all the ligaments to their respective parts according to quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone."
"But if it is displaced towards the hip joint, then the ligament attached to the bone should be stretched at the point of the penitent, so that the extension takes place there, and the other ligament on the other side of the fracture."
"And similarly if the fracture is displaced towards the bone end, then it should be done by stretching the ligament attached to the bone end, and the extension should take place on the other side of the parts."
"And therefore the doctor should press both sides of the bone inward so that it reduces according to its natural shape and the bone is joined together by good union. Then plaster and splinting should be applied if no inflammation occurs in the limb." "But if inflammation occurs, then allow that to rest for a few days until the inflammatory swelling subsides, and then return to your treatment."
"The splinting should be done as follows: wrap a hard bandage over the fracture and fold it two or three times, removing the excess; then wrap the limb so that it reaches to the heel and hip joint."
"And insert a long splint between the hip and the limb near its lower end; bring both ends of the splint above the upper part of the two parts, then wrap it around the limb and hip, removing any excess bandage."
"And then place a splint over the hip in the location of that fracture, and place another splint on the limb bone."
"And then fill the spaces between the limb and hip with soft cloths, so that the union is consolidated."
"And then begin with a strong splint on the middle of the limb over the location of the fracture with three or four layers. And as you go along with the ligament at the site of the fracture, make your splinting lighter and more flexible."
"And extend both ends of the splint to both sides of the fracture until it reaches the ankle bone, and strengthen both ends of the other splints on either side for fear that the ligament is dislodged from its place."
"And then leave the splint on for about three weeks until no inflammation occurs nor any heat or swelling appears. And if any cause of those occurs, then take care to remove it immediately and correct whatever has happened according to what we have said many times."
"But if the bone is broken at its tip, then you should reset it according to what is possible for you. And if not, then wait until it heals and then remove it, treating with whatever precedes the memory of it until it is healed."
"And sometimes this fracture of the hip joint is strengthened with those splints which are adjusted to it, as we have said in the assistant and in the arm."
"But however in the restoration of it in this manner it does not happen that the patient becomes lame. And if it is restored alone, without applying the other limb, then it is necessary that he limp on it at all times."
"And know that the hip joint is strengthened in forty days or a little more or less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions."
"Chapter 15."
"On the restoration of fractures of the patella."
"Know that a fracture of the patella is rare, but it often happens with a blow or fall."
"But if a fracture occurs to it, then it is only a crack or break in its parts, and this is with much pain or little pain. And over all this the diagnosis is made by sensation."
"And its restoration should be done by resetting whatever is broken in its parts with the said splints until it is united and joined according to what is possible for equitation, ease of construction."
"And then apply an emplaster and place a round splint if needed. And strengthen it above with a splint that is appropriate."
"And also consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in other fractures, such as inflammation and swelling, so that it opposes all accidents with whatever is appropriate for it until it is healed."
"Chapter 16."
"Of the restoration of a broken leg."
"Do you know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick and is called the shinbone; and the other is slender and is called the fibula?"
"And what happens to each of the types of fracture that happens to both bones of the arm also happens to it [the leg], and thus its restoration is like the restoration of the arm, and the operation for it is one."
"And if two bones are broken at once, the leg becomes completely useless."
"And if only the thinner bone is broken, the leg is paralyzed in front."
"And if only the thicker bone is broken, afterwards that one remains, and it is of those causes which are not lame."
"Therefore, extension, traction, and fixation of the splints should be applied according to that same manner."
"But nevertheless, if the fracture of the leg is such that it has many attempts, then extension should be less and lighter, and eased with your virtue."
"And in the leg there is some cause of operation on the arm, and this is when you align the splints and complete all your operation; then you will take pieces of wood from pine which is placed behind the causes which are placed below, and they should be made equally of branches of palm; lighten those which have less thickness, and do not use large ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, and from the knee up to the thigh."
"And therefore wrap each splint with two wrappings according to its length, and place one on the fracture of the leg from one side and the other from the other side, and they should be from the knee up to the ankle."
"And therefore fasten the two splints in three places of both extremities and in the middle place, because with this traction the leg is prohibited from bending at the right or left joint, and it remains in good position."
"And then apply a splint of wood according to the length of the leg; and let the leg be placed upon it so that it retains its movement."
"And especially when you do this in a fracture, and particularly when with that there is swelling properly."
"And therefore consider the leg each day and study it with that for final study, and keep it from suppuration and inflammation or other similar causes. And when any of those causes occurs, then pause there with what is necessary until it is healed."
Chapter xvii
Of the fracture of the ankle bone and the bones of the foot.
The ankle bone does not usually break completely, but the bones of the foot do sometimes fracture occasionally. And those of the foot rarely break, and they are only sprained in cases of disposition.
"And if any of the bones of the foot becomes fractured and those bones sometimes rise above each other, then the sick person places his foot on the ground prepared thus as when walking."
"And therefore pause the other foot upon what is raised from those bones, and press them down, squeeze them together, and keep them in their places until they return."
"And therefore apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small board at the bottom of the foot with two projecting heads."
"And therefore strengthen that with the leg by traction downwards since you have wrapped it with bandages and other means which are necessary."
"And when three or four days have passed, then undo the bandages, for you will find that the bones are united, either by being fixed together or reduced. And the operation on them is like those previously known."
"But if some of these are not fixed, then reduce them and fix them thus as you have seen in the reduction of the bones of the hand." "And therefore place a splint on the fractured finger according to its length, and let it be placed a little to the side of it. And then place that board which has been recommended under the foot, and strengthen it with traction downwards."
"But if two or three or more of the bones are broken, then place on each finger a splint according to its length wrapped in lion's skin cloth, and strengthen the board at the bottom of the foot, and let it stand upon one of the heads of the board with the sole of the foot for better retention."
"And it is necessary for you to remember that you do not neglect all the causes which have been mentioned to you concerning the other fractures which we have spoken of, and be prepared for every accident with what is necessary."
De la fractura del colze e dels osses de la man.
"Regarding fracture of the bone of the woman's pelvis, the bone of the penis, and the rod of man."
"When the bone of the woman is broken, then immediately cease that pressure in that area, and gently incline her to the side of the hip, keeping it held behind."
"And moreover the midwife will press the bone from outside, and it should be placed in the bone as if it were hoped; and moreover the woman should be moved gently, lifting her hips little by little, because all this is forgotten until it is before the opening of the bone as desired, and the broken bone returns."
"And moreover, powder should be placed on the hips, and when she wants to urinate, gently move the hip away with ease, and then she can urinate, and reduce it again according to how it was positioned at first, and then return to her initial powder."
"And do this for seven days or thereabouts, because that is when it will be restored."
"And if you wish, take an ovine bladder and stretch it over the bone of the hip using a reed cannula; then insert the entire bladder into the bone of the hip, and blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated within the bone, because this will cause the fracture to return."
"And then fill it again with cotton and keep it there for some days as we have recommended, until it is healed."
"And when the bone of the penis in a man or woman is broken, then you should apply the same treatment and stitching as we have recommended for the hipbone."
"And that which is right is not concealed in these fractures, nor is it difficult to find, especially for those who study and use this my book; because this fracture indicates to you the major dispositions of a straight line in restoring and repairing it, and therefore understand well."
"But in the case of a broken penis in a man, take a gourd of cucumber and place the penis inside it; then apply a bandage made of cloth over it and wrap it up, and leave it for three days or thereabouts, until it is healed."
"Chapter XIX."
"Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when they are with plague."
It is appropriate for one who has experienced a fracture with contusion, especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or arm, or similar, you should perform bloodletting immediately if conditions for phlebotomy are favorable as mentioned above.
And if the contusion flows with blood, it is necessary that you cauterize the wound immediately, powdering it with the above-mentioned mixture, unless there is another cause present.
And therefore begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this process if there are no signs of gangrene.
But if gangrene is present, delay restoring the fracture for up to nine days until the gangrene has subsided. Do not approach it on the third and fourth day altogether, for you will cause further harm.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, it is necessary that you perform reduction and alignment of it with your hands with ease and minimal extension.
But if extension is not prepared and the bone cannot be aligned with your hands alone, then use this tool: it is made of iron, seven or eight fingers long, and its width varies depending on the size of the wound. Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these tools ready, according to what is needed in the treatment of all kinds of fractures.
And once it is reduced, apply slight pressure to prevent it from being displaced during the operation.
And if there is an acute angle at its ends, cut it with the scissors near its extremities; and make sure that the upper part of the bone is thicker and the inner part is wider.
And this is how it looks:
And in Greek, it is called "labre volentz atelarem," which means "slight spatula."
So that the acute end may be elevated above the prominence of the bone.
And press it into place within a splint until the bone returns to its alignment with some reduction.
Then strive to align the ends of the fracture with one another. If the end of the broken bone is thin and does not fit well with the tool, then insert the tool between them so that it can have some leverage.
But if you cannot reduce the bone with all that has been recommended, then cut it with scissors in the manner that is described above. Or saw it with one of the saws, whichever way is easiest for you.
And remove any sharp edges or small rough spots from the remaining bone.
And when the bone has been reduced and the patient feels intense pain upon movement, you should know that the bone has not returned to its proper place. Therefore, be careful so that you do not cause further harm to the patient.
When you have completed the reduction of the bone, then cover it with a bandage soaked in black stiptic wine. And especially if it is summer, do not apply any ointment containing oil or other grease, so that it does not cause inflammation and corruption afterwards.
And apply bandages in the final stages of the reduction of the bone. Leave the dressing uncovered so that you can insert splints and use wrapping according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to strain the dressing at the fracture site, as many foolish doctors have done this and caused harm to their patients, resulting in death or gangrene. And if your patient's fracture is light, soft, different from other fractures.
And if the wound is large or infected and you fear that one of the bad accidents mentioned earlier may occur, causing pain in the area, then do not apply bandages. Instead, use a dressing made of thick cloth and tie it on firmly to keep the wound clean.
And after one or two days have passed since the wound began to suppurate, then remove the bandage that you had placed over it with wine. And apply poultices, as is customary in our practice for treating wounds, such as teriapharmacon and similar remedies.
And it is your duty to remove the splints and examine the wound every morning and evening until it has healed and is firm. And the limb should be positioned in such a way that pus can flow easily from it towards its lower parts.
If many days have passed and the wound is not consolidated nor has it begun to heal, then you should know that here are signs of pus formation. Therefore, explore the wound with a probe. And of those signs that are separated and removed or drawn out, if they are not separated from one another and put the limb in danger and cause pain, then persevere in incising them according to what you find possible.
And if pus or gangrene occurs in the wound, which is called stomatitis, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat it with one of those remedies that are described in their chapter under the heading of treatment.
And regarding that in which my speech should be brief and you perform your task: when the thick bone is crushed, do not attempt traction or extraction of it, as many times this has caused death. Leave it there until it grows together, for it will usually unite by itself in about 20 to 30 days. And then treat the wound if you see fit, or leave it alone.
Chapter XX.
Of the treatment of union that occurs in the place of some fracture.
Many times this condition occurs during the healing process of a fracture, especially near joints, and it is characterized by a deformity of the limb. It is strictly forbidden to move the limb from its natural position.
Then consider if the deformity is recent and administer those medicines that have astringent properties such as alum, resins, and myrrh. Take some of them and mix with stiptic wine, albumen of egg white, or vinegar, then apply it on the deformity wrapped in linen cloth and secure it with good compression.
And let the bandage remain undisturbed for several days until the swelling subsides. Then remove it and repeat the application until the deformity is removed; or place over that a lead plate descended from above, as lead has the property of reducing any swelling in the limbs.
If the deformity has already hardened and tightened, it may be necessary to remove it carefully. To do this, first make an incision along the superior edge of the limb and then incise the superfluous skin and fat until the hardened tissue is exposed. Then carefully remove that tissue using some probes or hooks until it is completely removed; afterwards, dress the wound until it heals.
"The chapter twenty-one."
"When a fracture has healed, but the limb remains thin and does not follow its natural course."
"When a bone fracture has healed, but the limb remains thin and does not follow its natural course, this happens for several reasons: one of which is the abundance of solution in the ligaments and bonds, not according to what is appropriate; or due to excess tightening of the ligaments preventing nutrients from reaching the limb; or due to excessive use of ointments; or due to unnecessary movement; or due to lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness."
"And its treatment is to feed the patient and make their heart strong so that blood may be increased in them; and administer baths; and apply warmth, joy, and comfort upon them."
"And also to apply plaster around the limb, so that the plaster draws nutrient to it, or continuous application of hot water, so that the nutrient flows and returns to its natural form."
"Chapter XXII."
"On the treatment of broken bones that have healed crookedly and why surgery is not appropriate in this case."
"When it happens that a limb which has already healed becomes crooked and prominent at the site of the break, and this causes the limb to appear misshapen, although the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, you should not listen to those who claim that the limb has been recently broken."
"But many foolish doctors and bone-setters have done this in our land, and this operation is most reprehensible and brings great fear upon those who bear its mark, which is death."
"But if the limpness and nodosity are recent, then they should be treated with hot water containing herbs that soften them, such as marshmallow and rosewater, and mallow, and similar ones; and a plaster made from white lead should be applied with a moistening plaster like litharge well-made; or gum tragacanth of rosewater mixed with wool grease or camphor oil, and this should be plastered upon it; or ripe figs crushed with dove's feathers, and similar to those medicines called softeners."
"And sometimes it is dissolved by frequent gentle friction with the most humid ones, and the member should be moved in all parts at all hours."
"But if the crookedness has already become hard and converted into bone and surgery is necessary, then it is appropriate to cut away what is superfluous from the prominence of that bone with subtle chisels, and to administer ease in that place with care and diligence."
"Chapter XXIII."
"On discourse concerning dislocation."
"Dislocation is the displacement of some of the joints from their place so that they are hindered in movement and cause deformity of the limb and bring about great pain and harm."
"Therefore when it happens that there is dislocation somewhere, then it should be promptly reduced and not delayed in reducing, because if it is delayed the joint becomes inflamed and firm, making reduction of dislocation difficult."
"And therefore one should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it brings about spasm and severe pain."
"But when it happens, then you should apply the cautery to the affected part and let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then embrocate the limb with hot water and oil, and reduce it gently."
"And every limb should be treated with that remedy which brings back the memory of its place."
"I have already set the bones of dislocation according to what is known about fractures; I placed the upper part of the body into the lower part."
"Chapter Twenty-four."
Of dislocation treatment of the jaw on the left side
If both mandibles are dislocated but do not reduce on their own, there are two methods to treat the dislocation of both jaws:
"However, if the dislocation of the limb is minor, it often returns to its proper position on its own accord."
"If the dislocation is entire and complete, then it must be reduced gently, without delay."
"And the assistant should hold the patient's head, while the doctor inserts one finger of his right hand into the dislocation of the jaw within the patient's mouth if it is on one side; or inserts both fingers together if the dislocation is on two sides. The others present should hold the rest of the body firmly in place and command the patient to keep their jaws still and relax those muscles that cause each part to move separately. Then the doctor gently moves the jaw back into its proper position."
"And if the reduction of the dislocation is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then steam should be administered with hot water and oil to facilitate gentle and easy reduction without delay."
"After the dislocation has been reduced and the jaws are in place, the patient's mouth should be closed gently and not forced open. Then powdered cloth with cerate should be applied to facilitate easier reduction, using melted wax and rose oil."
"And the patient should sleep on their back with their head supported between two pillows to prevent turning it to either side. They should not be forced to chew anything, but have liquid food until the pain has subsided and the jaws are healed, after which they may eat what they wish."
"And this should be administered with care to avoid any strain on the opening of the mouth during eating and drinking until the jaw is healed."
"And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated and cannot be returned to their proper places, then frequent vomiting, fever, and constant thirst often occur, with severe abdominal pain and sometimes bloody diarrhea."
"And when this happens, you should know that it is a serious condition; many times the patient dies within ten days."
Captulo XXV
**Of the reduction of dislocation of the clavicle and the extreme end of the muscle.**
The shoulder blade is not displaced from its position due to its connection with the chest; sometimes it can be displaced outward, and this is apparent by touch.
To restore it, the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms. Then press tightly with your palm for a moment, as it will return to its place.
Then apply plaster and powder there and bind it firmly.
However, if the extremity that is attached to it is not displaced on any day, then it should be reduced and bandaged according to what has been said and according to your judgement.
Then apply plaster, powder, and dressing there, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
With this same treatment, the detached end of the muscle is also reduced.
Chapter XXVI
Of the reduction of dislocation of the muscle
You should know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways: one is that it is dislocated at the attachment of the tendon to the upper arm bone, another is at the attachment of the tendon to the elbow.
And the second way is that it is dislocated at the attachment above the muscle, and this is rare; and it is not dislocated at the attachments behind by the shoulder blade or in front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and exits at the attachments below the elbow near the tendon of the biceps, especially in those individuals whose flesh is thin, it is easily dislocated; but in those with much flesh, it exits out with difficulty.
And sometimes it happens to some men that through a blow or fall the muscle becomes swollen with blood and clotted, and is then dislocated by that swelling.
Therefore you should remove this [swelling] before it becomes completely hardened, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation of it is recognized when it occurs at the attachments below the elbow near the tendon of the biceps; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, since there is a manifest difference between them. And you find on the head of the muscle a concavity towards the elbow and the tendon of the biceps feels like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move it in any way.
If it is dislocated at the lower end or above, then you can immediately find a palpable swelling which is not hidden.
And this dislocation undergoes easy reduction when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner elevates the patient's hand towards above; and approaching from behind he places his thumbs below the tendon of the biceps of that arm, and elevates the joint with force up to its place, and then the practitioner raises the patient's hand, extends it upwards; and while doing so he should pause it below when it returns easily.
But if it does not return by what has been said, and especially at the beginning when the dislocation occurred many days ago, then the patient should be immersed in hot water and given a poultice that softens and lubricates like a common liniment made from althea root, fenugreek, and marshmallow leaves in water.
And then the patient should be propped up on his back and placed below the elbow with his arm suspended between elevation and tension, and the physician places his heel upon the sphere [of the joint], elevates the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not hidden at its lower attachments; and another practitioner should hold the patient's head so that it is not moved from its place, for then it quickly returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, you can also reduce it in this way: have present someone who is taller than the patient and stands on that side; let him insert his muscle behind the tendon of the dislocated arm and elevate the tendon upwards while the patient is suspended in the air; then another practitioner pulls the patient's hand from its lower attachments towards his belly. But if the patient is light in weight, then it is necessary that some other cause be suspended from him in order to weigh him down, for the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is also reduced in another way: this is by placing on the ground a long stake whose head is round like that of a mortar pestle, neither too thick nor too thin. Then place the patient on top of the head of the stake with cloths beneath him; then let the patient stand along the length of the stake and extend his arm at its lower attachments towards the other side, while extending his body on the other side with force so that the joint quickly returns to its proper position.
"If reduction proves difficult for all the reasons mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a stick whose length is that of two arms, and whose width is four fingers, and its thickness should be eased in the middle according to this form:"
"And similarly, a lion's skin should be laid over the patient's head so that the stick does not slip."
"And then the stick should be placed on top of the patient's titillic, and their hand or arm should be extended over the stick to either side, and the stick should be bound over the assistant's hands and the arm and the tip of the hand."
"And then place your arm on a ladder rung according to its width, and extend your hand to either side, and the other end should be suspended from the other side, for the joint will be ready soon."
"And when the treatment is completed according to each possible manner, then you should place a compress made of wool of equal quantity on top of the titillic; and then apply an emplaster prepared from powder of ground almonds and egg whites over the entire muscle, just as it is rolled up, and then tighten beneath the titillic with moderate tension; and the bandage should be wrapped around the emplaster, and his hand should be attached to his collar, and he should rest it for seven days without moving his hand."
"And the patient's food should be limited during this time while the member is recovering, for it is easier for healing to occur."
"And then food should be gradually increased after seven or eight days and some movement exercised; and thus if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed."
"But if the joint is often dislocated due to humidity that occurs there or for some other cause, then cautery should be administered in that place with three burns, as is mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery."
"And even if all of this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed but the joint remains loose, and the emplaster and tension are applied to it several times without effect, and he cannot raise that arm above his head, then know that the nerves of that muscle, which are at its head, have been injured or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be put back in its proper place."
"For the dislocation of that which has occurred, you should press the patella and the kneecap or the parts behind, and the reduction is made with pressure and extension using both hands until it returns."
"And then apply the other bandage to that place and cure it until it is healed."
"And if there is heaviness in the member that occurs during healing and slowness of its movement, then the patient should use hot baths several times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original nature."
"Chapter XXVII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the coccyx."
"You should know that the joint of the coccyx is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but more so towards the front than the back. The dislocation of it is not hidden from you for you can see it clearly by looking or touching according to whatever figure it has been dislocated, and when you reduce the displaced coccyx with your hand, that is made clear to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch its own muscle."
"And you should know that it is necessary to reduce the dislocation immediately before an abscess appears, for if an abscess appears, reducing it is difficult and the healing will not be complete; and especially when it is in the back parts, for there is no worse species of dislocation nor greater pain, and several times death results from it."
"And the reduction of it, when that which is possible to reduce occurs, is that his hand should be extended with two hands and his arm spread out, and the physician's hands should be placed on the coccyx from below, and he pushes the joint back with the force of both hands together or with the heel of his palm until it returns to its place."
"But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it returns thus by bending his hand with his own hand until he strikes the coccyx with the heel of his palm."
"And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then apply powerful extension and great force."
"And let two assistants extend the arm and hold it firmly with both hands so that it is not moved during the extension."
"And then turn the arm in every direction, because it is wrapped up with a doubled cloth or bandage around his hand."
"And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil for this purpose that this may be one of those causes which help in lubricity of the joint with facility. And then push the joint back with powerful pressure until it returns."
"And then the reduction of it should be supported with that plaster in which there is astringency and suction with white of egg, and it should be extended with descending bandage, and his arm should be suspended from his neck, and he should rest for some days."
"And then let it be untied. And if the joint is formed in its proper place, then you should untie the bandage from it and leave it alone."
"But if you see that the joint is not strong, then repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is extended. And then untie it."
"But if heaviness occurs during the reduction of the joint and its position is in movement, then apply moistening in baths and gentle friction and compression until it is relieved. Or let him rest upon the joint elevated, for this is a matter of fatty motion. And then let him be bound, and leave it on overnight." "And then remove that and put him in a bath. And when he rises, then friction the joint with gentle friction. And then repeat over it elevated, for this is routine of fatty motion, once, twice, three times." "Within the bath until it is softened."
"And if you wish, apply over it the hollow of a moist cow's horn scarified on its edges and press upon that. Do this several times for it is moist and reduces to its position."
"Chapter XXVIII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of maltreatment, that is of the joint which is between the arm and the hand."
"Dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs and its reduction is easy, unlike other joints. But nevertheless it is necessary that there should be swiftness in the reduction of it at the time when it is dislocated before a tumor appears or something happens with an abscess."
"And the reduction of the dislocation of it is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and an assistant extends his hand. And the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its proper place."
"But nevertheless it is necessary to consider if the palm has already been removed from the surface of the hand. Then the patient places the back of his hand on a table for extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is anterior, then the position of the patient's hand should be internally rotated upon the table so that the hand of the physician may lie over that prominence."
If it does not return quickly, then press it with an embrocation that draws out the abscess and leave it. And do not repeat this unless necessary, for otherwise it will suffer and you cannot reduce the inflammation that passes through the days.
But first soften the joint according to its tortuosity; and do not notify the patient of any cause, but say that your hand is softened and cannot press any cause. Then you will know that the nerves are crushed or cut, and therefore it is necessary to cauterize them near their outlet, and afterwards tell him some cause.
Then when it is returned badly, then pause on the embrocation which we have recommended, and let it be laid and pressed for 5 days. And near about it should be untied and left alone, and the hand of the patient should be exercised.
But if the movement of the hand is difficult and it occurs to him some heaviness, then you should apply that with cold water and friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX
Of the treatment of dislocation of the said (joints).
Sometimes these are dislocated entirely out of their place.
And when some finger is dislocated from those on the side of the palm or on the back of the hand, then stretch the finger and push back the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
And then support the tip of the finger, and suspend that above the part where it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it can be covered with its bandage on that same day.
"And when it is night, lie down according to that narration, and do not cease loosening him during the day and exercising with movement, and lie down with him at night. And do this for several days until it becomes tight."
"And do likewise if it is dislocated in the stomach or hand. Lie down according to that partition and perform your first operation until it is healed."
"And similarly do this when it is dislocated at other joints."
"Chapter XXX."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the spine."
"When complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the spine or neck, removing the displaced vertebra from its place is not a cure, for death overtakes the sick person."
"And this is indicated by the fact that the evacuation of the sick person's excrement is without control and he cannot retain it. Many times some members become relaxed on one side or both sides, or both arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place, for many times removal occurs and many times the displacement to all four parts is made."
"Therefore that which has already been removed to the posterior parts is called sciatica (gelbozitat)."
"But the treatment of it is this: first consider whether sciatica occurs in childhood, for if it does, there is no cure nor healing for that."
"But those that occur from falling or striking or similarly to these have already been discussed by the Ancients with lengthy words, of which the majority returns with profit; but this is now abbreviated because it is too long for me and excuses itself from it due to my proximity to the intention of my exposition, with which I have explained and formed diverse instruments with which I will show this and explain that."
"And of these only those that occur properly in the back can be cured by what I will recount."
"And it is that the sick person should be laid on his back with his head close to a wall, and extended outwards from him by means of soft padding so that the foot of him does not get hurt."
"And properly a straight stick should be placed and set on the ground at the end of the foot around his head, and another stick on the other side of his feet at the other end of the foot."
"And the minister should hold the stick, and not be fixed in a hole; and another minister should hold the other according to that form."
"And properly he should cover the head of the sick person and tie him there tightly with linen bandages and fasten them; and extend the end of the bandage to the stick that is above his head, and tie it there; and properly stretch another bandage over his hips on both sides of him, and above his knees; and properly adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick that is above his feet."
"And properly each minister should extend the bandage at the beginning of the stick and not remove the two sticks and the bandages from their places where they are set, but nevertheless not fixed as we have said."
"And the doctor should place his palms on the sick person's back with force so that he returns, or rather if he places one table on it and properly adjusts his feet on the table so that he returns."
"But if he does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is around three braccia and hollow out in the wall that we have said is near the sick person a place in which to fit the end of the table." "And properly place the middle of the table on his back and the doctor places his feet on the other end and strongly presses so that the back is shortened and returns to its proper place."
"And if you wish, open it with a turn made by hand. And it is that you place yourself on the ground near the head of the sick person at the end of his foot between two sticks whose length is one braccio and at the end of his head similarly two sticks; and let the distance between each stick be one palm and already made in each stick a hole through which it turns, and let each one be firmly fixed on the ground so that they are not moved."
"And let there also be placed a round stick on which is the hole and it should be turned in the two holes of the two sticks."
"And at its end there should be a hole through which the stick is passed whose length is one palm with that which turns. And in the other two sticks there should be similar ones."
"And properly tighten the bandages that you have tightened on his foot at the hole that is above his head and the bandages that you have tightened on his thighs at the hole that is below his feet."
"And properly above each hole there should be a minister who turns the hole with his hand and the doctor presses on the back as we have said."
"And properly when the back has returned and the place is pressed, then it is necessary to place an absorbent plaster with alum of hawk and properly cotton; and properly place on the plaster a table whose width is around three digits or about that, and its length should be equal to the extent of the swelling, and on the beginning of the back."
"And properly tie it with the bandage we mentioned and feed the sick person a subtle diet until he is healed."
"And if there remains some inflammation in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and cleanse with administration by table for a long time which we have recommended. And already administered in that place lead plate."
"And sometimes inflammation occurs at the end of the two spine bones and it is squeezed by that dislocation. And that bone already threatens such that it should not be operated on with this medicine, for strong rubbing will cause you to die."
"Chapter LXXXI"
"On the treatment of a dislocated hip."
"You should know that the dislocation of the hip and the dislocation of the muscle is not only a displacement, but also an injury which does not occur in other joints with small movement and concavity."
"The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways: it can be dislocated inward or outward, forward or backward."
"Most commonly, the hip joint is dislocated inwardly, and less commonly forward or backward."
"The sign of hip dislocation inward is that when you press the healthy thigh against the affected one, it feels longer and the kneecap seems more prominent than on the healthy side. The patient cannot bend their leg backwards and must have an incision to drain the hematoma."
"The sign of hip dislocation outward is that the injuries occurring in it are opposite to this one."
"The sign of anterior hip dislocation is that the thigh expands according to its nature but does not bend due to pain in the knee area. If forced to do so, it cannot extend forward. The urine is retained and there is a hematoma in the groin area. Treatment should be directed towards the heel."
"The sign of posterior hip dislocation is that the knee does not expand and cannot be bent forward. The thigh appears shorter than the other one, the groin area is softened, and treatment should be directed towards that area."
"However, reduction of these types of hip dislocations should be considered with caution if the dislocation is old and chronic. In such cases, one should administer traction according to the patient's condition, but this is not the only treatment option. In these cases, there is no need for forced manipulation."
"However, in cases of recent dislocation that fall into one of the four types, it is necessary to reduce the joint and extend the leg inward and outward. Then move the leg to the right and left until it returns to its normal position, as this alone will suffice."
"But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong rope and extend the thigh as far back as possible. Alternatively, you can attach ropes to the upper part of the leg or use a sling placed over the thigh above the knee. Another assistant should extend their hand above the patient's foot so that their fingers point towards the toes. Then, grasp one end of the rope tightly with your left hand while another assistant holds the other end."
"And extend it either from the inside to the angle of the pubis or from behind to the buttock area."
"And let all of this happen in one glance, while the patient is lifted up on their own body from the earth and remains hanging."
"This type of extension is common to all four (limbs)."
"And therefore if the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be good; but otherwise, now it is necessary to speak of its proper cure."
"In the reduction of its own (joint), when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is inwards at the hip joint, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their side with the affected leg; and then pause the limbs above the root of the thigh at that point where the head of the thigh bone enters the socket; and then extend the limb upwards from the angle of the socket to the furthest possible part of the clavicle."
"And then another person should take hold of the limb firmly and press the thick part of the dislocated joint and extend it forcefully outward, because that will return it to its proper place."
"And this type is of easier reduction with which this limb is reduced than the other types."
"But if it is excused on you and does not obey the entrance with this type of reduction, then both feet of the patient should be lifted together with firm, light pressure, on both heels and both toes."
"And let the length of each (foot) be by the amount of four digits, and let the affected limb be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers."
"And then the patient should be suspended over the head of the bed, with their back up, and let the length from the ground be by the amount of two cubits."
"And then command a strong youth to pull down the head of the joint, and let another youth suspend it, and the other youth pull down the joint with forceful impetus, because the articulation returns to its proper place with speed."
"But in the proper reduction, when there is dislocation outward at the joint, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their back according to what we have recommended in the event of stiffness; and let the limb be straightened over the affected joint properly and over its foot."
"And then two sticks should be placed, one at the foot of it, and the other at its head; and let two sticks be placed that are adjusted in the middle of the elbow, over which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not injured because the stick is between their joints, for the extension to the joints should not be forced."
"And then each assistant should extend on his own side, and the physician with his hand guide the reduction according to the art."
"When dislocation is at the front parts, then it is necessary that the bed be extended lightly, and this is according to this method; and the doctor places the palm of their right hand on the sick person's flank, and then grips that with the other hand, and with that grip extends the legs away from the leg of the pain."
"If the dislocation is at the back parts, then it is not necessary that the sick person be extended with the legs away from them, and they are raised off the ground; it is necessary that they be placed on a hard surface, like this: if the hip joint is dislocated externally, according to what we have said about placing it on two widths; and they lie on their face and the limbs are extended, as we have said."
"It is necessary that compression be administered with a board over the location where the joint is dislocated."
"So when the reduction is completed as it should be - and the sign of the reduction of the hip is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the sick person's two thighs, and when you feel those joints and the sick person extends their thigh and expands that without excuse, then you know that the member has been returned to its proper place; then you apply two splints and bind it tightly with a bandage so that the joint is not moved in any of the parts; and let the sick person rest for three days or four."
"And after removing the ligature and splint, compare one thigh with the other."
"Then if you feel those joints firmly, then you know that the dislocation is already healed, and therefore let the sick person go."
"And if you see any sign of weakness, then return and splint that again, and extend it according to your first method, and let it rest for three days."
"And after removing the splint, let them walk with support on crutches for a few days until they are strengthened."
"Chapter XXXII."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder blade."
"The shoulder blade is dislocated in three ways: for it is dislocated at the outer parts, and at the inner parts, and more often at the upper and rear parts; but it is not dislocated at the front."
"And the sign of dislocation of the shoulder blade is that when you tell the sick person to apply their thigh to the hip, they are unable to do so."
"And the reduction of all kinds of dislocation is as follows: make the sick person lie on their right side, so that they can extend their thigh if possible; press their knee against the outer face of the shoulder blade, and place their foot between your feet; then apply both palms to the shoulder blade of the sick person, and press firmly with your elbows over the shoulder blade of the sick person while another assistant extends their foot until the shoulder blade returns to its proper place."
"And the sign of reduction is that the thigh adheres to the hip with ease and effortlessly."
"And then splint it and make the thigh adhere to the hip, then bind them together with a bandage for three days or four, then remove the splints."
"Then let the sick person not walk until they are healed and strengthened."
"But if you cannot reduce it, then apply strong traction with ligatures as described in the treatment of hip dislocation."
"Chapter XXXIII."
"Treatment of an ankle dislocation."
"The talus bone may sometimes be displaced by minor trauma, and sometimes it becomes completely dislocated, with dislocation occurring either internally or externally."
"And the sign of talus bone dislocation is that sometimes the talus becomes swollen at the joint where it has dislocated."
"The treatment for removing it is easy reduction; it should be extended gently with hands and gradually returned."
"Furthermore, in the treatment of it when it is displaced according to completion, you should make the patient lie on their right side, and have a strong and able servant hold them firmly by the hips from behind."
"And appropriately, place your right hand on the upper part of their thigh and your left hand lower down towards their knee."
"And properly pull the leg towards you with your right hand, and appropriately grasp it twice with your left hand, and then extend it with your left hand; and press firmly on the joint of the leg with your right hand without force."
"And perform this procedure twice as we have discussed."
"And then press firmly on the joint of the leg with your hand for the third time; you are now at the knee."
"So if it returns in one or two attempts with this method, and the leg feels right again, that is good. But if not, then repeat the procedure until it returns."
"But if you are prohibited from performing the reduction with this method that we have recommended, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a sturdy stick firmly on the ground at the foot of their bed. Cover them with a blanket so that they do not move."
"And then the minister of the church should hold their things, and another minister should extend the leg with both hands or with some cloth placed on the sole of the foot; and each minister should extend themselves against extension of the hip, so that the stick is extended between the patient's thighs retaining them, so that the body of the patient is not pulled towards the sides during extension."
"And then the doctor should reduce the dislocation with their hands, and another minister should hold the healthy joint on both sides, for the dislocation returns easily."
"And then once the dislocation has returned and you have declared that the reduction is successful, then apply plaster and bind it firmly with cloth, and tie the leg with cloth at the joint."
"And be careful or guard yourself from the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the cloth does not press too tightly and hurt them."
"And then leave him for two days or three; and if the cloth becomes moistened, replace it."
"Then on the third or fourth day, untie it and forbid the patient from vomiting for 40 days." "If it is necessary to go ahead with this space, it's not certain that the dislocation isn't already dissolved and corrupted, precisely because of this."
"If an abscess forms after cautery, you must administer to it what was mentioned earlier in this story regarding cure and embrocation, until it is removed."
"Chapter XXXIV."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the said hips."
"It is necessary that he who has been dislocated be stretched out with slight extension without moving him; and if it is difficult, let him be lifted up easily."
"But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the foot of the leg, then place the patient's leg over a low stool or table, and let him be standing thus as if walking."
"And then stand on the foot and pause his leg over what you have prepared from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and see if it is now aligned and does not appear in the prominence of the joint."
"And then place the lower leg of the thigh on a table that takes in all the leg, with two heads, and then stretch it with appropriate and firm traction for three days; and then untie him and have him rest from walking for more days until he is strengthened and recovery is assured."
"Chapter XXXV."
"On the types of dislocation that are made with pain or fracture or with each one separately."
When some cause occurs from that, and you strive in its cure and restoration, many times death succeeds.
However, it is not necessary to presume over its cure, but rather that one who is knowledgeable in the art, experienced, merciful and compassionate, cautious, not hasty or presumptuous; and who uses medications at the beginning that only cause apostemas cauterizations, leaving the patient's fate to divine judgment, unless there is hope for salvation from destruction, relief from illness, and some sign of improvement appears. Then make haste to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes an apostema caut.
And then if the limb returns as desired, afterwards administer regimen which causes cauterization of apostema and heals the plague with what is convenient for it of the remedies for drying humidity.
And if dislocation with fracture occurs in the limbs and bones separated, then you perform the reduction of them, and do in this what we have said in the simple maladies of that which is preceded in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that which enters through the paths of fear according to what is preceded by my instruction; for this is better for you to owe to your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthful to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Al-Bukaysis perfectly, of which God be blessed. Amen.
